مأذون فيه هو السليم دون غيره عرفاً وعادة فيضمن وفي المحيط : ولو تخرق لتقصيره في العمل ولعدم معرفته بالعمل يضمن عندنا وعند زفر . وقيد بقوله بعمله» فشمل عمله بنفسه وعمل أجيره لأنه عمله حكماً قال في المحيط : ثم الأجير المشترك. إنما يضمن ما تلف في يده بشرائط ثلاثة : الأول أن يكون في قدرته دفع ذلك الفساد فلو لم يكن له قدرة على ذلك كما لو غرقت السفينة من موج أو ريح أو جبل صدمها لا ضمان على الملاح. الثاني أن يكون محل العمل مسلماً إليه بالتخلية فلو لم يكن محل العمل مسلماً إليه بأن كان رب المتاع في السفينة أو وكيله فانكسرت السفينة بجذب الملاح لم يضمن. وأما الثالث وهو أن يكون المضمون مما يجوز أن يضمن بالعقد فلو استأجر دابة لحمل عبد صغير أو كبير فلا ضمان على المكاري فيما عطب من سوقه أو قوده قال في المحيط : لو تلف من فعل أجير القصار لا متعمداً فالضمان على القصار لا على الأجير لأن التلف حصل من عمل القصارة، ولو وطئ ثوباً فتخرق ينظر ؛ إن كان يوطاً مثله لا ضمان عليه لأنه مأذون ،دلالة، وإن كان لا يوطأ بأن كان رقيقاً ضمن. ولو وقع من يده سراج فاحرق ثوباً من القصارة أو حمل شيئاً فوقع على ثوب القصارة فتخرق فالضمان على الأستاذ . ولو استأجر رجلاً ليخدمه فوقع شيء من يده من متاع البيت ففسد لا يضمن، ولو وقع الأجير على ثوب وديعة عند الأستاذ فتخرق ضمن الأجير لأنه ليس بمأذون فيه . وذكر في الأصيل : انفلتت المدقة من يد الأجير فأصابت شيئاً فضمانه على القصار، ولم يفصل بين ثوب القصارة ،وغيره ومشايخنا فصلوا فقالوا : إن وقع على ثوب الوديعة ابتداء وخرقة ضمن الأجير، وإن وقع على ثوب القصارة ابتداء يضمن الأستاذ دون الأجير لأنها انفلتت ابتداء على ثوب الوديعة فهذا عمل غير مأذون فيه فيضمن، فأما إذا انفلتت على ثوب القصارة ابتداء فهو عمل مأذون فيه الأجير فيضمن الأستاذ، وعلى هذا التفصيل إذا أصاب آدمياً وقالوا : لو مشى الضيف على بساط المضيف فتخرق من مشيه لم يضمن لأنه مأذون فيه وكذا لو انقلبت الأواني فانكسرت بخلاف ما إذا وطئ آنية من الأواني فأفسدها يضمنها لأنه ليس بمأذون فيه . ولو جفف القصار ثوباً على حبل فمرت حمولة فخرقته فالضمان على الحمال والراعي إذا ساق الغنم فمات أو وطئ بعضها بعضاً فمات إن كان أجيراً مشتركاً ضمن ، وإن كان أجيراً خاصاً فلا ضمان عليه ا هـ . مختصراً. وقوله «من دقه أي دقه حقيقة أو حكماً كدق أجيره. وقوله كزلق «الجمال قال في الجامع الصغير : استأجر جمالاً ليحمل له كذا إلى موضع كذا فزلق الجمال في أثناء الطريق؛ إن حصل بجناية يده ضمن وإن حصل بما لم يمكن الاحتراز عنه لا يضمن عند الإمام وعندهما يضمن. وفي الذخيرة : هذا إذا تلف في وسط الطريق، ولو زلقت رجله بعد ما انتهى إلى المكان المشروط فله الأجر ولا ضمان عليه وهو قول محمد أخيراً، وعلى قوله أولاً يضمن هنا أيضاً. وفي الولوالجية : ولو مطرت السماء فأفسدت الحمل أو أصابته الشمس ففسد فلا ضمان على قول الإمام، وعند أبي يوسف يضمن. وفي الأصل: استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها شيئاً فعثرت الدابة فوقع الحمل أو المملوك لا يضمن المملوك ويضمن الحمل قالوا: إنما يضمن المتاع إذا كان الصبي لا يصلح لحفظ المتاع لأنه لو كان يصلح له لا يضمن المتاع، ولو مر بالدابة على قنطرة وفيها حجر أو ثقب فوقع فيه حمله فتلف يضمن. وقيد بزلق الجمال المستأجر لأنه لو لم يستأجره. قال في المحيط : استأجر قدراً فلما فرغ حمله على حمار فزلق رجل الحمار فوقع فانكسر القدر، فإن كان الحمار يطيق حمل ذلك فلا ضمان عليه، وإن كان لا يطيق فإنه يضمن ا هـ . قوله وانقطاع الحبل الذي يشد به الحمل» قال محمد في الأصل: إذا انقطع حبل الجمال وسقط الحمل وتلف ضمن قيد بقوله «يشد به الحمل» لأنه لو كان الحبل لصاحب المتاع لا يضمن. قال العناية : ولو حمل بحبل صاحب المتاع فتلف لم يضمن وقال في الهداية وقطع الحبل من قلة اهتمامه فكان من صنعه. ولقائل أن يقول : تقدم أن الأجير المشترك لا يضمن ما تلف في يده إن كان الهلاك بسبب يمكن الاحتراز وفرق بأن التقصير هنا في نفس العمل فيضمن وهناك في نفس الحفظ فلا يضمن. ولو قال رب المتاع للحمال احمله فحملاه فسقط لم يضمن لأن التسليم إليه لم يتم، ولو حمله ثم استعان في موضعه برب المتاع فوضعه فتلف ضمن عند أبي يوسف ولم يضمن عند محمد، ولو قال احمل أيهما شئت هذا بدرهم وهذا بنصف درهم فحملهما فله نصف أجرهما ونصفها إن هلكا، ولو حمل أحدهما أولاً فهو متطوع في الثاني ويضمنه إن هلك لأنه حمله بغير إذن. ولو استأجره ليحمل له جلود ميتة فوقعها وأتلفها فلا أجر ولا ضمان لأنه ليس بمال، ولو استأجره ليحمل هذه الدراهم إلى فلان فأنفقها في نصف الطريق ثم دفع مثلها إلى فلان فلا أجر له لأنه ملكها بأداء الضمان وفي الواقعات: استأجره ليحمل كذا في طريق كذا فأخذ في طريق آخر تسلكه الناس فتلف لم يضمن قوله وغرق السفينة من مدها. أطلق في قوله من مدها فظاهره أنه يضمن سواء كان رب المتاع أو لم يكن وليس كذلك. قال في الأصل: الملاح إذا أخذ الأجرة وغرقت السفينة في موج أو مطر أو فزع وفي الخانية أو من شيء وقع عليها أو من شيء ليس في وسعه دفعه فلا ضمان عليه وإن حصل الغرق من أمر يمكن التحرز عنه فكذلك عند الإمام، وعندهما يضمن وإن حصل الغرق من مده وصاحب المتاع لم يضمن. وفي الأصل: وإن كان صاحب المتاع في السفينة أو وكيله وغرقت السفينة من مده ومعالجته فلا ضمان إلا أن يخالف بأن يضع فيها شيئاً أو يفعل فيها فعلاً متعمداً الفساد وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أجرت الدابة فسقط المتاع فهلك وصاحب المتاع معه فإن الأجير يضمن ا هـ. والمراد بالمد حبل السفينة التي تمد به . وفي التتمة: استأجر سفينة ليحمل عليها الأمتعة هذه فأدخل الملاح عليها أمتعة أخرى بغير رضاه وغرقت وهي كانت تطيق ذلك لم يضمن الملاح ا هـ. قال
Book of Hire, Chapter on Liability of the Employee

What is permitted is sound (goods) without others customarily and by custom, so he is liable. And in al-Muhit: If it tears due to his negligence in work or due to his lack of knowledge of the work, he is liable according to us and according to Zufar. And he restricted with his saying 'by his work' so it includes his own work and the work of his employee, because it is his work by legal implication. He said in al-Muhit: Then the shared hired worker is only liable for what perishes in his hand under three conditions: First, that it be within his ability to prevent that damage; if he had no ability for that, as if the ship sinks from waves or wind or a mountain striking it, no liability on the sailor. Second, that the place of work be handed over to him by vacating; if the place of work was not handed over to him, as if the owner of the goods is in the ship or his agent, and the ship breaks due to the sailor's pulling, he is not liable. And the third is that the thing guaranteed be something that can be guaranteed by contract; so if he hires a donkey to carry a young or old slave, no liability on the carrier for what is damaged from its hitting or leading. He said in al-Muhit: If destruction occurs from the action of the fuller's hired worker unintentionally, the liability is on the fuller, not on the hired worker, because the destruction resulted from the work of fulling. And if he steps on a garment and it tears, it is considered: if it is normally stepped on, no liability on him because it is impliedly permitted; if it is not normally stepped on, as if it is delicate, he is liable. And if a lamp falls from his hand and burns a garment from the fuller's, or he carries something and it falls on a fuller's garment and tears, the liability is on the master. And if he hires a man to serve him and something falls from his hand from the household goods and spoils, he is not liable. And if the hired worker falls on a garment held in trust (wadi'a) with the master and tears it, the hired worker is liable because it is not permitted. And it is mentioned in al-Asil: The pestle slipped from the hired worker's hand and hit something; its liability is on the fuller, and he did not distinguish between the fuller's garment and others. And our scholars distinguished, saying: If it falls initially on the wadi'a garment and tears it, the hired worker is liable; if it falls initially on the fuller's garment, the master is liable, not the hired worker, because it fell initially on the wadi'a garment, so this is an unauthorized action, so he is liable; as for when it falls on the fuller's garment initially, it is an authorized action for the hired worker, so the master is liable. And according to this distinction if it hits a human. And they said: If a guest walks on the host's carpet and it tears from his walking, he is not liable because it is permitted. Likewise if vessels tip over and break, contrary to if he steps on a vessel and spoils it, he is liable because it is not permitted. And if the fuller dries a garment on a rope and a load passes and tears it, the liability is on the porter. And a shepherd, if he drives sheep and they die or some trample others and die, if he is a shared hired worker he is liable, if he is a private hired worker, no liability. End, summarized.

And his saying: 'from his pounding' i.e. his pounding literally or legally like the pounding of his hired worker. And his saying: 'like the slipping of the camel' He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: He hired a camel driver to carry something for him to a certain place, and the camel slipped on the way: if it occurred due to his hand's wrongdoing he is liable, and if it occurred due to something that could not be guarded against, he is not liable according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is liable. And in al-Dhakhira: This is if it perishes in the middle of the road; but if his foot slipped after he reached the stipulated place, he gets the wage and no liability, and this is Muhammad's later opinion; according to his earlier opinion he is liable here too. And in al-Walwalijiyya: If it rained from the sky and spoiled the load or the sun hit it and it spoiled, there is no liability according to Imam, and according to Abu Yusuf he is liable. And in al-Asl: He hired a donkey to carry something on it, and the donkey stumbled and the load or slave fell; the slave is not liable and the load is liable. They said: He is only liable for the goods if the boy is not fit to guard the goods, because if he was fit for it, he would not be liable for the goods. And if he passed the donkey over a bridge and there was a stone or a hole in it, and his load fell into it and perished, he is liable. And it is restricted to the slipping of the hired camel driver because if he did not hire him... He said in al-Muhit: He hired a cooking pot; when he finished, he loaded it on a donkey; the donkey's foot slipped and it fell and the pot broke; if the donkey could bear that load, no liability on him; if it could not bear it, he is liable. End.

His saying: 'and the breaking of the rope with which the load is tied' Muhammad said in al-Asl: If the camel driver's rope breaks and the load falls and perishes, he is liable. It is restricted by his saying 'with which the load is tied' because if the rope belonged to the owner of the goods, he is not liable. al-Inaya said: If he carried with the owner's rope and it perished, he is not liable. And in al-Hidaya: The breaking of the rope is due to lack of his care, so it was from his action. And one might say: It was mentioned that the shared hired worker is not liable for what perishes in his hand if the destruction was due to a cause that can be guarded against, and the difference is that negligence here is in the work itself, so he is liable; there it is in the preservation itself, so he is not liable. And if the owner of the goods said to the porter: Carry it, and he carried it and it fell, he is not liable because the delivery to him is not complete. And if he carried it then sought help from the owner at the spot and he placed it and it perished, he is liable according to Abu Yusuf and not liable according to Muhammad. And if he said: Carry whichever you wish, this for a dirham and this for half a dirham, and he carried both, he gets half of the wage for both, and half if they perish. And if he carried one first, he is a volunteer for the second and is liable for it if it perishes because he carried it without permission. And if he hired him to carry dead animal skins for him, and he dropped them and destroyed them, no wage and no liability because they are not property. And if he hired him to carry these dirhams to so-and-so, and he spent them halfway then gave similar ones to so-and-so, he has no wage because he owned them by paying the guarantee. And in al-Waqi'at: He hired him to carry something on a certain road, and he took another road that people travel, and it perished, he is not liable.

His saying: 'and the sinking of the ship from its rope' He made absolute in his saying 'from its rope', so its apparent meaning is that he is liable whether the owner of the goods is present or not, but it is not so. He said in al-Asl: The sailor, if he took the fee and the ship sank in waves, rain, or panic (and in al-Khaniyya, or from something that fell on it, or from something he cannot prevent), no liability on him. And if the sinking occurred from something that could be guarded against, it is likewise according to Imam, and according to the two he is liable. And if the sinking occurred from his rope and the owner of the goods was not in the ship, he is not liable. And in al-Asl: If the owner of the goods was in the ship or his agent, and the ship sank from his rope and his handling, no liability unless he violates by putting something in it or doing an action intentionally causing damage. And this is contrary to the case if he rented a donkey and the goods fell and perished while the owner was with it, then the hired worker is liable. End. And the meaning of 'mad' is the rope of the ship with which it is pulled. And in al-Tatimma: He hired a ship to carry these goods on it, and the sailor brought other goods onto it without his consent, and it sank while it could bear that, the sailor is not liable. End. He said.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding the rulings on the liability of the hired worker (ajir). The author discusses the shared hired worker (ajir mushtarak) who works for multiple clients. The main rule is that the ajir mushtarak is liable for damage only if he is negligent or exceeds the permitted scope. The passage first states that damage due to negligence in work or lack of skill leads to liability, even according to Zufar. It then lists three conditions for liability from al-Muhit: (1) the worker must have been able to prevent the damage, (2) the work must have been handed over (takhlia) to him, and (3) the damaged item must be something that can be guaranteed by contract. Examples follow: if a ship sinks due to natural causes, the sailor is not liable; if the owner is present, the worker is not liable because the place was not vacated. The condition that the item must be valuable (mal) is illustrated by hiring a donkey to carry a slave; if the slave is damaged, the carrier is not liable because slaves are not considered property in this context? Actually, the text says 'no liability on the carrier for what is damaged from its hitting or leading' – meaning if the slave is injured by the donkey's action or leading, the carrier is not liable because slaves are not subjects of guarantee in such contracts? This needs careful reading. Then the passage gives various scenarios: a fuller's worker stepping on a garment, a pestle slipping, a guest walking on a carpet, etc. In each case, liability depends on whether the action was customary and thus implicitly permitted. For instance, if a pestle initially falls on a garment held in trust (wadi'a), the hired worker is liable because it is unauthorized; if on a fuller's garment, the master is liable because it is authorized. The text also discusses the case of a camel driver (jammal) whose camel slips: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the slip is unavoidable, no liability; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), liability in all cases. This difference recurs in other contexts like rain damage or ship sinking. Later, the passage covers the breaking of a rope: if the rope belongs to the employer, the worker is not liable; if it belongs to the worker (or is his responsibility), he is liable because the break is due to his negligence. Other cases include a porter dropping a load, spending entrusted money, taking a different road, and a sailor who overloads a ship without permission. In all these, the key factors are: authority (idhn), custom, and ability to prevent damage. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position often follows Imam Abu Hanifa, but the text notes differences and some later opinions of Muhammad.

Key Terms
أجير مشترك (ajīr mushtarak): A hired worker who is not exclusively employed by one person, working for multiple clients; liable for damage only under specific conditions of negligence or unauthorized action.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage, arising from contract or tort.
مأذون فيه (ma'dhūn fīh): An action that is permitted (by custom or explicit permission), thus no liability for incidental damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition is a shared hired worker (ajir mushtarak) NOT liable for damage to goods?
A) When the damage was due to his negligence.
B) When the damage was caused by something beyond his ability to prevent (e.g., natural disaster).
C) When he acted without the owner's permission.
D) When the goods were not valuable.

Key Takeaway
The liability of the ajir mushtarak hinges on whether the damage was preventable and within his authorized scope.

Liability of Hired Beast Handler When Beast Stumbles
Ruling on liability for goods and animals when hired beast stumbles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a hired beast stumbles, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the condition of the beast's capacity to determine liability.

Core Idea
A hired beast handler guarantees goods only if the beast cannot bear the load—liability hinges on capacity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a common scenario in ijarah: a hired beast stumbles and goods are damaged. The Hanafi school has an early disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the final ruling is conditioned on whether the beast could bear the load. Understanding this condition is key to applying the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a hired beast stumbling while carrying goods. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds no liability, while Abu Yusuf holds liability. Note the pronoun 'al-Imam' refers to Abu Hanifa.
The text begins with 'ضمان على قول الإمام' – this is Abu Hanifa's position.
2
Locate the ruling from al-Aṣl
The base text from al-Aṣl states: if the beast stumbles, the hired worker does not guarantee a slave (or child) but does guarantee the goods (load). The reasoning given is that the child's fitness to guard matters.
'لا يضمن المملوك ويضمن الحمل' – the slave is not guaranteed, the load is.
3
Extract the causal condition ('illa)
The 'illa is the capacity of the beast or the fitness of the child. If the child could hold on, no liability; if not, liability. For goods, the condition is whether the beast could bear the load. This is confirmed by the pot-on-donkey case from al-Muḥīṭ: if the donkey could bear the load, no guarantee; otherwise, guarantee.
The 'illa determines liability: capacity = no liability; incapacity = liability.
4
Apply the condition to similar cases
Use the condition to evaluate other scenarios, such as the pot broken on a slipping donkey. The test is always the same: was the beast capable of carrying the load under normal conditions?
The passage ends with a stipulation that this applies only when the handler was hired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمان على قول الإمام، وعند أبي يوسف يضمن. وفي الأصل: استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها شيئاً فعثرت الدابة فوقع الحمل أو المملوك لا يضمن المملوك ويضمن الحمل قالوا: إنما يضمن المتاع إذا كان الصبي لا يصلح لحفظ المتاع لأنه لو كان يصلح له لا يضمن المتاع، ولو مر بالدابة على قنطرة وفيها حجر أو ثقب فوقع فيه حمله فتلف يضمن. وقيد بزلق الجمال المستأجر لأنه لو لم يستأجره. قال في المحيط : استأجر قدراً فلما فرغ حمله على حمار فزلق رجل الحمار فوقع فانكسر القدر، فإن كان الحمار يطيق حمل ذلك فلا ضمان عليه، وإن كان لا يطيق فإنه يضمن ا هـ .
Guarantee according to the Imām (Abu Hanifa) [says no], and according to Abū Yūsuf he guarantees. And in al-Aṣl: He hired a beast to carry something, then the beast stumbled, and the load or the slave [on it] fell: he does not guarantee the slave but he guarantees the load. They said: He only guarantees the goods if the child is not fit for guarding the goods because if he were fit for it, he would not guarantee the goods. And if he passed with the beast over a bridge and in it there was a stone or a hole, and his load fell into it and perished, he guarantees. And it is restricted because of the slipping of the hired camel-driver, because if he had not hired him... [incomplete]. He said in al-Muḥīṭ: He hired a pot, then when he finished, he carried it on a donkey, and the donkey's leg slipped, so it fell and the pot broke. Then if the donkey could bear that load, there is no liability, and if it could not bear it, then he guarantees. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on the liability of a hired worker (ajir) when a beast of burden stumbles and goods are damaged. The passage opens with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imām Abu Hanifa holds that the worker is not liable (ضمان على قول الإمام), while Abū Yūsuf holds that he is liable. Then the text quotes from al-Aṣl (a foundational work) a detailed case: a hired beast stumbles, causing the load or a slave to fall. The ruling distinguishes: the slave (or child) is not compensated because it is a human, but the goods are. The reasoning ('illa) is that if the child is capable of holding on, the worker is not liable; if not, he is liable. The text adds that if the beast passes over a bridge with a hazard (stone or hole) and the load falls, the worker is liable, as he should have avoided it. A restriction is made: this liability applies only if the helper (camel driver) was hired; if un-hired, different rules apply. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Muḥīṭ: a similar case where a pot is carried on a donkey that slips. The key condition is the donkey's capability: if it can bear the load, no liability; if not, the worker is liable. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that the hired worker is liable only when his action is the cause of damage and he failed in the duty of care.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage.
أجير (ajir): A hired worker, either exclusive (khass) or common (mushtarak).
عثرت (atharat): To stumble or trip, referring to the beast's action.
يطيق (yutiq): To be capable or strong enough to bear a load.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Muḥīṭ, if a hired donkey carrying a pot slips and breaks the pot, when is the handler NOT liable?
A) When the donkey was overloaded.
B) When the donkey could bear the load.
C) When the pot was valuable.
D) When the handler was not present.

Key Takeaway
The key condition for liability is whether the beast could bear the load; if capable, no guarantee.

Liability for Rope Breaking: Worker's vs. Owner's Rope
Ruling with proof on rope-breaking liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability when a hired worker's rope breaks and distinguish it from when the rope belongs to the owner.

Core Idea
Liability depends on rope ownership: worker's rope = his negligence; owner's rope = no liability.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding liability when the rope used to tie a load breaks, causing the load to fall and be damaged. The key factor is whose rope was used—if the hired worker provided the rope, it indicates negligence in his work and he must compensate, but if the rope belonged to the owner, there is no liability. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi distinction between different types of hired workers and the nature of negligence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage gives two cases: the worker's rope breaks vs. the owner's rope breaks. Note the different rulings for each.
The ruling is found in Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Asl.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is from Muhammad in al-Asl and al-Hidayah: the rope breaking is attributed to lack of care, making it the worker's own action.
3
Analyze the distinction (tafriq)
The passage distinguishes between negligence in the work itself (taqsīr fī nafs al-'amal) and negligence in preservation (taqsīr fī al-ḥifẓ). Understand why the former leads to liability and the latter does not.
4
Explore the hypothetical objection
The passage considers an objection based on the general rule that a common worker is not liable for destruction that could be avoided. Understand how the Hanafi response resolves it.
The objection is answered by the distinction between types of negligence.

Source Text

قوله وانقطاع الحبل الذي يشد به الحمل» قال محمد في الأصل: إذا انقطع حبل الجمال وسقط الحمل وتلف ضمن قيد بقوله «يشد به الحمل» لأنه لو كان الحبل لصاحب المتاع لا يضمن. قال العناية : ولو حمل بحبل صاحب المتاع فتلف لم يضمن وقال في الهداية وقطع الحبل من قلة اهتمامه فكان من صنعه. ولقائل أن يقول : تقدم أن الأجير المشترك لا يضمن ما تلف في يده إن كان الهلاك بسبب يمكن الاحتراز وفرق بأن التقصير هنا في نفس العمل فيضمن وهناك في نفس الحفظ فلا يضمن.
His statement: 'And the cutting of the rope with which the load is tied.' Muhammad said in al-Aṣl: If the camel driver's rope breaks and the load falls and perishes, he guarantees, restricted by his statement 'with which the load is tied' because if the rope belonged to the owner of the goods, he would not guarantee. Al-'Ināyah said: And if he carried with the owner's rope and it perished, he does not guarantee. And in al-Hidāyah: And the cutting of the rope is due to lack of care, so it is from his own action. And it can be said: It has preceded that the common hired worker does not guarantee what perishes in his hand if the destruction was due to a cause that can be avoided, and the distinction is that the negligence here is in the work itself, so he guarantees, while there it is in preservation, so he does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability when the rope used to secure a load breaks, causing damage. The key point is ownership of the rope: if the hired worker provided the rope, he is liable because his lack of care caused the break; if the rope belonged to the owner, the worker is not liable. The commentary from al-Hidāyah emphasizes that the rope breaking shows negligence in the work itself (taqsīr fī nafs al-'amal), distinguishing it from cases where the damage occurs due to a failure in preservation, which does not incur liability for a common worker. This illustrates the Hanafi distinction between different types of hired workers and the level of responsibility.

Key Terms
انقطاع الحبل (inqitā' al-ḥabl): The breaking of the rope used to secure the load.
قلة اهتمام (qillat ihtimām): Lack of care or negligence.
أجير مشترك (ajīr mushtarak): A common hired worker who works for multiple people.
تقصير في نفس العمل (taqsīr fī nafs al-'amal): Negligence in the work itself, e.g., using a faulty rope.
تقصير في الحفظ (taqsīr fī al-ḥifẓ): Negligence in preservation, e.g., failing to guard the goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, in which case is the hired worker liable for damage from a rope break?
A) When the rope belongs to the owner and breaks due to wear.
B) When the rope belongs to the worker and breaks due to his lack of care.
C) When the rope breaks regardless of ownership.
D) When the rope belongs to the worker and breaks due to a hidden defect unknown to him.

Key Takeaway
The hired worker is liable for damage caused by the breaking of his own rope because it stems from negligence in his work, but not for the owner's rope.

Porter's Liability When Owner Helps Carry
Rulings on porter liability with owner participation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a porter's liability when the owner participates in carrying, distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and explain the role of delivery (taslim).

Core Idea
No liability if delivery incomplete; after delivery, help may shift liability per imam.

In contracts of hire (ijara), the porter's liability for damage depends on whether the goods were fully delivered to him. This lesson examines two scenarios where the owner participates: first, helping to carry from the start, and second, helping to place after the porter has carried. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on the second scenario, which we will analyze.

Guided Path
1
State the basic rule of liability for a hired porter
A hired porter (ajir) is liable for damage only after he takes full possession (taslim) of the goods. Before that, the owner retains responsibility.
This is based on the principle that the hired worker is an amana (trustee) until delivery; thereafter he becomes a guarantor for negligence.
2
Apply to scenario 1: owner helps carry from start
Here the owner physically participates in carrying, so the goods were never solely in the porter's control. Hence delivery (taslim) is incomplete, and the porter does not guarantee if the item falls.
3
Apply to scenario 2: porter carries, then asks owner to help place
The porter had already taken possession (completed delivery), but later asks the owner to assist in placing. The dispute centers on whether this assistance nullifies the porter's responsibility.
Abu Yusuf treats the request as negligence, making the porter liable; Muhammad sees the owner's act as a new intervention breaking the chain.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds the porter liable (ḍāmin) because he should have completed the task himself. Muhammad holds he is not liable (lā yadman) because the owner's act is a separate cause of damage.
Both opinions are recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah; no explicit tarjih is given in this text.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the item perishes, Abu Yusuf requires the porter to compensate the owner; Muhammad does not. A mufti must know which view is relied upon in his region.
The rest of the passage continues with other scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال رب المتاع للحمال احمله فحملاه فسقط لم يضمن لأن التسليم إليه لم يتم، ولو حمله ثم استعان في موضعه برب المتاع فوضعه فتلف ضمن عند أبي يوسف ولم يضمن عند محمد، ولو قال احمل أيهما شئت هذا بدرهم وهذا بنصف درهم فحملهما فله نصف أجرهما ونصفها إن هلكا، ولو حمل أحدهما أولاً فهو متطوع في الثاني ويضمنه إن هلك لأنه حمله بغير إذن.
And if the owner of the goods said to the porter: 'Carry it,' and they both carried it, and it fell, he does not guarantee because the delivery to him was not completed. And if he carried it, then sought help in its place from the owner, and he placed it, and it perished, it is guaranteed according to Abū Yūsuf but not according to Muhammad. And if he said: 'Carry whichever you wish: this for one dirham and this for half a dirham,' and he carried both, then he gets half of both wages and half of them [i.e., half the value of each if they perish]. And if he carried one first, then he is a volunteer in the second and guarantees it if it perishes because he carried it without permission.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage addresses several scenarios of a porter's liability. First, if the owner helps carry the item and it falls, the porter is not liable because the item was not fully handed over to him. Second, if the porter carries the item and then asks the owner to help place it, and the item is damaged, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abū Yūsuf holds the porter liable, Muhammad holds he is not. Third, if the owner gives the porter a choice between two items at different rates, and the porter carries both, he is entitled to half the agreed wages for each, and if both are lost, he is liable for half their value. Finally, if the porter carries one item first and then the second, he is a volunteer (mutatawwi') for the second and is liable for it if lost because he carried it without specific permission.

Key Terms
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery or handing over of the goods to the worker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when the owner and porter both carry the item and it falls?
A) The porter is liable.
B) The owner is liable.
C) The porter is not liable because delivery was not completed.
D) They share liability.

Key Takeaway
A porter's liability hinges on whether delivery (taslim) is complete; after delivery, help may trigger a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Porter's Liability for Carrying Multiple Items with Choice and Sequence
Rulings on wage and liability with multiple items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the porter's wage and liability when the owner offers a choice between items, and state the ruling when the porter carries items sequentially.

Core Idea
Choice between items halves wage and liability; sequential carrying makes second item volunteer status.

This lesson covers two additional scenarios from the same passage: first, when the owner gives the porter a choice between two items with different wages, and the porter carries both; second, when the porter carries one item first and then a second. The first scenario involves proportional wage and liability, while the second introduces the concept of a volunteer (mutatawwi') who is liable for loss.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario of choice between items
The owner says: 'Carry whichever you wish: this for one dirham, this for half a dirham.' The porter carries both. Since he chose both implicitly, he is entitled to half the wage of each item, and if both perish, he must pay half the value of each.
The wage is halved because he only followed one of the two alternative offers for each item; by carrying both, he accepts both offers partially.
2
Apply the principle of compensation
If the items perish, the porter compensates half the value of each. This matches the wage: he is entitled to half the wage, so he bears half the risk.
This is based on proportionality: the wage and liability are aligned.
3
Analyze the sequential carrying scenario
If the porter carried one item first, then later carried the second without being hired for it, he is a volunteer (mutatawwi') for the second. As a volunteer, he is liable if it perishes because he carried it without the owner's explicit permission.
The first item was under a valid hire; the second is unauthorized.
4
Identify the basis of liability for the volunteer
A volunteer (mutatawwi') is considered a usurper (ghāṣib) in effect: he takes possession without right. Therefore, he guarantees the item if it is lost, even without negligence.
This is stricter than a hired porter, who is only liable for negligence after delivery.
5
Note the contrast with the first scenario
In the choice scenario, the porter carried both items with at least implicit permission for both, so he is not a volunteer. Hence his liability is proportional. In the sequential scenario, the second item lacked permission, triggering full liability.

Source Text

And if the owner of the goods said to the porter: 'Carry it,' and they both carried it, and it fell, he does not guarantee because the delivery to him was not completed. And if he carried it, then sought help in its place from the owner, and he placed it, and it perished, it is guaranteed according to Abū Yūsuf but not according to Muhammad. And if he said: 'Carry whichever you wish: this for one dirham and this for half a dirham,' and he carried both, then he gets half of both wages and half of them [i.e., half the value of each if they perish]. And if he carried one first, then he is a volunteer in the second and guarantees it if it perishes because he carried it without permission.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage addresses several scenarios of a porter's liability. First, if the owner helps carry the item and it falls, the porter is not liable because the item was not fully handed over to him. Second, if the porter carries the item and then asks the owner to help place it, and the item is damaged, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abū Yūsuf holds the porter liable, Muhammad holds he is not. Third, if the owner gives the porter a choice between two items at different rates, and the porter carries both, he is entitled to half the agreed wages for each, and if both are lost, he is liable for half their value. Finally, if the porter carries one item first and then the second, he is a volunteer (mutatawwi') for the second and is liable for it if lost because he carried it without specific permission.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): Volunteer, acting without reward or formal contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sequential scenario, why is the porter liable for the second item if it perishes?
A) Because he was negligent.
B) Because he carried it without permission.
C) Because he was hired for it.
D) Because the owner asked him to.

Key Takeaway
When a porter carries multiple items, the basis of permission determines wage and liability: implicit permission halves both; no permission triggers full volunteer liability.

Liability for Carrying Dead Skins and Dirhams, and Route Changes in Ijarah
Rulings on liability for non-property items, replacement of dirhams, and route change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on three scenarios in an ijarah contract: carrying non-property items, using and replacing dirhams, and changing the route, and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Dead animal skins are not property; using dirhams transfers ownership; changing to a common route is not a breach.

In an ijarah (hiring) contract, the subject matter must be valuable property (māl). What happens if the hired worker carries something that is not considered property, or if he uses the goods midway, or if he takes a different route? This lesson examines three rulings from the Hanafi school that clarify the limits of liability and wage entitlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three distinct situations: (1) carrying dead animal skins, (2) carrying dirhams and using them midway, (3) changing the route to a commonly used road.
These scenarios test the boundaries of the ijarah contract's subject matter and worker's actions.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For each scenario, state whether the worker receives wages and whether he is liable for loss: (1) no wage, no liability; (2) no wage; (3) no liability.
Note that in the dirham case, the text explicitly denies wage but does not mention liability – the guarantee is paid, so liability is moot.
3
Analyze the rationale
Identify the ʿilla (legal cause) for each ruling. For dead skins: they are not māl. For dirhams: the worker owns them by paying the guarantee (adā' al-ḍamān). For route change: taking a customary road is not a breach of trust.
The rationale flows from the nature of the subject matter and the worker's actions.
4
Apply the general principle
Synthesize the underlying principle: The subject of ijarah must be valuable property; any action that fundamentally alters or replaces the subject voids the contract (no wage); taking a customary alternative path does not invalidate the contract.
This principle applies across similar cases.
5
Consider the authority
Note that these rulings are from the Zahir al-Riwayah (the first two) and al-Wāqiʿāt (the third), indicating their weight in the Hanafi school.
Zahir al-Riwayah represents the primary transmitted position; al-Wāqiʿāt records later practical cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجره ليحمل له جلود ميتة فوقعها وأتلفها فلا أجر ولا ضمان لأنه ليس بمال، ولو استأجره ليحمل هذه الدراهم إلى فلان فأنفقها في نصف الطريق ثم دفع مثلها إلى فلان فلا أجر له لأنه ملكها بأداء الضمان وفي الواقعات: استأجره ليحمل كذا في طريق كذا فأخذ في طريق آخر تسلكه الناس فتلف لم يضمن
And if he hired him to carry dead animal skins for him, and he dropped them and destroyed them, then there is no wage and no guarantee because they are not property. And if he hired him to carry these dirhams to so-and-so, and he spent them halfway, then gave similar ones to so-and-so, then he has no wage because he owned them by paying the guarantee. And in al-Wāqī‘āt: He hired him to carry something on a certain road, so he took another road that people use, and it perished: he does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage covers two unique cases. First, carrying dead animal skins (which are not considered valuable property in Hanafi law, as they are najis and not saleable); the worker neither receives wages nor is liable for compensation. Second, carrying dirhams: if the worker uses the money midway and then replaces it, he does not get wages because by paying the guarantee he effectively owns the dirhams, so the contract is void. The third case from al-Wāqi‘āt involves changing the route: if the worker takes a different road that is commonly used, and the goods perish, he is not liable, as long as the new route is not harmful.

Key Terms
جلود ميتة (julūd mayyita): Skins of dead animals (carrion), considered non-property (not māl).
أداء الضمان (adā' al-ḍamān): Paying compensation for the consumed dirhams, thus owning them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a hired worker carries dead animal skins and they are destroyed?
A) Wage and liability
B) No wage but liability
C) No wage and no liability
D) Wage but no liability

Key Takeaway
In ijarah, the subject matter must be valuable property; if it is not, the contract is invalid, and the worker does not receive wage or bear liability for loss.

Liability of a Boatman: Avoidable vs Unavoidable Causes
Ruling on boatman's liability for sinking due to towing and external causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on boatman's liability for ship sinking and distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding avoidable causes.

Core Idea
The boatman is not liable for unavoidable causes, but the Sahibayn hold him liable for avoidable causes, while Imam Abu Hanifa does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the liability of a boatman when a ship sinks. The key distinction is the cause of the sinking: natural forces beyond control or human action that could have been avoided. The text also reveals an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether the boatman is liable even for avoidable causes. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the law of hire (ijara) in maritime contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the boatman's operation of the ship for hire. The ruling on liability depends on whether the sinking is from an unavoidable or avoidable cause.
The default position is no liability for natural causes.
2
Classify the cause of sinking
Determine if the cause is unavoidable (e.g., wave, rain, falling object) or avoidable (e.g., negligence in towing).
Unavoidable causes are those the boatman cannot repel.
3
Apply the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For avoidable causes: Imam Abu Hanifa says no liability; Sahibayn say liability. For unavoidable causes: all agree no liability.
The text also discusses scenarios where the owner is present and whether the boatman violated the contract.
4
Consider additional scenarios
The text provides rulings when the owner is on board, or when the boatman adds extra goods without consent. These modify the basic rule.
Violation of contract may impose liability even if cause was avoidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وغرق السفينة من مدها. أطلق في قوله من مدها فظاهره أنه يضمن سواء كان رب المتاع أو لم يكن وليس كذلك. قال في الأصل: الملاح إذا أخذ الأجرة وغرقت السفينة في موج أو مطر أو فزع وفي الخانية أو من شيء وقع عليها أو من شيء ليس في وسعه دفعه فلا ضمان عليه وإن حصل الغرق من أمر يمكن التحرز عنه فكذلك عند الإمام، وعندهما يضمن وإن حصل الغرق من مده وصاحب المتاع لم يضمن. وفي الأصل: وإن كان صاحب المتاع في السفينة أو وكيله وغرقت السفينة من مده ومعالجته فلا ضمان إلا أن يخالف بأن يضع فيها شيئاً أو يفعل فيها فعلاً متعمداً الفساد وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أجرت الدابة فسقط المتاع فهلك وصاحب المتاع معه فإن الأجير يضمن ا هـ. والمراد بالمد حبل السفينة التي تمد به . وفي التتمة: استأجر سفينة ليحمل عليها الأمتعة هذه فأدخل الملاح عليها أمتعة أخرى بغير رضاه وغرقت وهي كانت تطيق ذلك لم يضمن الملاح ا هـ. قال
His statement: 'And the sinking of the ship from its towing.' He spoke absolutely concerning 'from its towing,' so its apparent meaning is that he guarantees whether the owner of the goods was [present] or not, but it is not so. He said in al-Aṣl: The boatman, if he takes the wage and the ship sinks in a wave, or rain, or panic, and in al-Khāniyya, or from something that falls upon it, or from something that is not in his capacity to repel, then there is no liability. And if the sinking occurs from something that can be avoided, then similarly according to the Imām [there is no liability], but according to the two [Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad] he guarantees. And if the sinking occurs from his towing and the owner of the goods does not [help], [he does not guarantee? unclear]. And in al-Aṣl: If the owner of the goods or his agent was in the ship and the ship sank from his towing and his handling, then there is no liability unless he violates [the contract] by putting something in it or doing an act intentionally corrupt. This is contrary to the case when a beast is hired and the goods fall and perish while the owner is with it, for the hired worker guarantees. End. And the meaning of 'al-madd' is the rope of the ship with which it is towed. And in al-Tatimmah: He hired a ship to carry these goods on it, then the boatman brought other goods onto it without his consent and it sank, and [the ship] could bear that: the boatman does not guarantee. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This lengthy passage addresses the liability of a boatman (mallāḥ) when a ship sinks. The key distinction is the cause of sinking. According to al-Aṣl, the boatman is not liable for sinking due to natural causes beyond his control (waves, rain, panic). If the sinking is from a cause that can be avoided, the ruling differs: according to Imām Abu Hanifa, there is still no liability, but according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad), he is liable. The text also discusses the situation where the ship sinks from the boatman's own towing (madd) while the owner is present: no liability unless the boatman violates the contract. Finally, from al-Tatimmah, if the boatman adds extra goods without consent and the ship sinks, but it was capable of carrying the extra, he is not liable.

Key Terms
ملاح (mallāḥ): Boatman or sailor who operates the ship.
مد (madd): Towing rope; the rope used to pull the ship.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a boatman's liability when the ship sinks due to a wave?
A) He is liable because he took a wage.
B) He is not liable because it is an unavoidable cause.
C) He is liable only if the owner was not present.
D) He is liable for half the value.

Key Takeaway
The boatman is not liable for unavoidable causes; for avoidable causes, the Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa (no liability) and the Sahibayn (liability).

Liability for a Broken Jar (Dann) During Carriage
Ruling on broken jar during carriage with discussion of khiyar and intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for porter liability when a jar breaks in transit, distinguishing cases of action vs external cause, and the khiyar (choice) options.

Core Idea
A porter guarantees the jar's value if it breaks by his action, but not if caused externally; his wage is adjusted proportionally.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in the chapter of ijarah (hire): a porter hired to carry a jar, which breaks during transit. The ruling hinges on whether the break was caused by the porter's action (e.g., slipping) or an external force (e.g., crowd pushing). The Hanafi school provides detailed rules for guarantee and wage adjustment, with notable disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of breakage
First, determine if the jar broke due to the porter's own action (e.g., slipping) or due to external factors (e.g., being pushed by people). The ruling on guarantee and wage differs accordingly.
The text distinguishes between 'binafsihi' (by his action) and 'bi ghayri sana'ihi' (without his action).
2
Apply the rule for breakage by porter's action
If the porter caused the break (e.g., slipped), he guarantees the value at the break location and loses wage for the distance beyond that point. However, he has a choice (khiyar): he may consider himself a transgressor from the start (no wage, full guarantee) or only at the moment of break (wage for distance carried before break, guarantee only at break point).
The khiyar is only available when broken by his action; not when external cause.
3
Apply the rule for breakage by external cause
If external forces caused the break (e.g., crowd pushing), two views: Imam Abu Hanifa states no guarantee and no wage; the two Sahibayn hold that the porter guarantees value at break point with no choice, because the object is treated as guaranteed (mamnun) from the start.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is important for fatwa.
4
Consider the condition 'on the road'
The phrase 'on the road' (fi al-tariq) is a restrictive condition: if the jar breaks after reaching home, the porter receives full wage and no guarantee (Muhammad's later view). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's earlier view, he remains liable.
The later view is considered mufta bihi.
5
State the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position: if the porter's action breaks the jar, he guarantees value at break point and receives wage proportionally (with khiyar). If external cause, no guarantee (Imam's view). If break occurs after delivery home, no guarantee and full wage (Muhammad's later view).
This reflects the settled practice in Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن انكسر دن في الطريق ضمن الحمال قيمته في محله ولا أجر أوفى موضع الانكسار وأجره بحسابه أما الضمان فلأنه تلف بفعله لأن الداخل تحت العقد عمل غير مفسد والمفسد غير داخل فيضمن على ما بينا وأما الخيار فلأنه إذا انكسر في الطريق شيء واحد تبين أنه وقع تعدياً هذه الوجه، وله وجه آخر وهو الحمل حصل بأمره فلم يكن متعدياً وإنما صار تعدياً عند الكسر فيميل إلى أي الجهتين شاء، فإن مال إلى كونه متعدياً من الابتداء ضمنه قيمته ولا أجر ،له وإن مال إلى كونه مأذوناً فيه في الابتداء وإنما حصل التعدي عند الكسر ضمنه قيمته في موضع الكسر وأعطاه الأجر بحسابه. قال في شرح الطحاوي: معناه أسقط قدر الأجرة هذا إذا انكسر بصنعه بأن زلق وعثر، فإن عثر بغير صنعه بأن زحمه الناس لا يضمن عند الإمام ولا أجر له، وعندهما يضمن قيمته في موضع ما انكسر ولا يخير لأن العين مضمونة عندهما على ما بينا. قال في التتارخانية : هذا إذا انكسر الدن بجناية يده أما إذا حصل لا بجناية يده؛ فإن كان يأمر لا يمكن التحرز عنه لا ضمان عليه بالإجماع، وإن هلك بأمر يمكن التحرز عنه فكذلك عند الإمام، وعندهما يجب الضمان وللمالك الخيار. وقوله في الطريق قال في الذخيرة. قيد احترازي فإذا انكسر الدن بعد ما انتهى به إلى بيته فله الأجر ولا ضمان عليه وهذا قول محمد أخراً، أما على قول أبي يوسف وهو قول محمد أولاً يجب أن يكون ضامناً ! رحمه الله : (ولا يضمن حجام أو فصاد أو بزاغ لم يتعد الموضع المعتاد) لأنه التزمه بالعقد فصار واجباً عليه والفعل الواجب لا يجامعه الضمان كما إذا حد القاضي أو عزر ومات المضروب بذلك إلا إذا كان يمكنه التحرز عن ذلك كدق الثوب فأمكن تقييده بالسليم بخلاف الفصد ونحوه فإنه ينبني على قوة الطبع وضعفه ولا يعرف ذلك بنفسه ولا ما يحتمله الجرح فلا يمكن تقييده بالسليم وهو غير الباري فسقط اعتباره إلا إذا جاوز المعتاد فيضمن الزائد. وهذا كله إذا لم يهلك، وإن هلك يضمن نصف دية النفس لأنه هلك بمأذون وغير مأذون فيضمن بحسابه حتى لو أن الختان قطع الحشفة وهو عضو كامل يجب عليه الدية نصف الدية وهي من أندر المسائل وأغربها حيث يجب الأكثر بالبرء وبالهلاك الأقل. وفي شرح الطحاوي: لو قطع الحشفة فعليه القصاص، ولو قطع بعض الحشفة فلا قصاص عليه، ولم يذكر ما يجب عليه . وفي الصغرى : تجب حكومة عدل. وفي
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if a jar breaks on the road, the porter guarantees its value at its location, and there is no wage for the distance up to the break point, and he receives wage in proportion to [the distance carried]. As for the guarantee, it is because it perished by his action, because what is included under the contract is work that is not corrupting, and the corrupting is not included, so he guarantees based on what we have explained. As for the choice, because when a single item breaks on the road, it becomes clear that a transgression occurred in this manner, and there is another manner: the carriage occurred by his command, so he was not a transgressor, but the transgression only occurred at the break; so he may lean towards whichever of the two sides he wishes. If he leans towards it being a transgression from the beginning, he guarantees its value and there is no wage for him. If he leans towards it being permitted in the beginning and the transgression only occurred at the break, he guarantees its value at the break site and gives him wage proportionally. He said in Sharh al-Tahawi: Its meaning is that he deducts the amount of the wage. This is when it breaks by his own action, like slipping and stumbling. But if he stumbles without his own action, such as people pushing him, then according to the Imam he does not guarantee and there is no wage, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he guarantees its value at the place of break and there is no choice, because the object is guaranteed according to them as we have explained. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: This is when the jar breaks due to a wrong action of his hand. But if it occurs without a wrong action of his hand, then if it is something that cannot be avoided, there is no guarantee by consensus. If it perishes due to something that can be avoided, then similarly according to the Imam, but according to the two, guarantee is required and the owner has the choice. And his statement 'on the road' — he said in al-Dhakhira: it is a restrictive condition. Thus if the jar breaks after he has reached his house, he gets the wage and there is no guarantee, and this is the later view of Muhammad. However, according to Abu Yusuf and the earlier view of Muhammad, he should be liable. May Allah have mercy on him: 'And a cupper, a phlebotomist, or a leecher does not guarantee if they do not exceed the customary area' because he undertook it by contract, so it became obligatory upon him, and an obligatory action does not coexist with guarantee, just as when a judge imposes a fixed punishment or discretionary punishment and the person dies from it, except when it is possible to guard against it, like beating a garment, so it becomes possible to restrict it to safe [areas], unlike phlebotomy and the like, because it depends on the strength or weakness of the constitution and he does not know that by himself, nor what the wound can bear, so it cannot be restricted to safe [areas] and it is non-customary, so its consideration falls away, unless he exceeds the customary area, then he guarantees the excess. This all if he does not die. But if he dies, he guarantees half the blood-money of the person because he died due to that which is permitted and that which is not permitted, so he guarantees proportionally. So much so that if a circumciser cuts the glans, which is a complete organ, he must pay half the blood-money, and this is one of the rarest and strangest issues where a larger amount is owed for healing and a smaller for death. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he cuts the glans, there is qisas; if he cuts part of the glans, there is no qisas, and he did not mention what is required. In al-Sughra: An equitable compensation is required. And in
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on two topics: (1) liability when a jar (dann) breaks while being carried by a porter, and (2) liability of medical practitioners like cupper (hajjam), phlebotomist (fassad), and leecher (bazzagh). For the jar, the key is whether the break occurred due to the porter's action (e.g., slipping) or external causes. If due to his action, he guarantees the value at the break point and loses wage for the distance carried beyond that point; he has a choice (khiyar) to decide whether he was a transgressor from the start or only at the break, affecting wage. If due to external causes like people pushing, Imam Abu Hanifa says no guarantee and no wage, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he guarantees value at break point with no choice. The condition 'on the road' is restrictive: if the jar breaks after reaching home, the porter gets full wage and no guarantee (Muhammad's later view). For medical practitioners, they are not liable for harm if they stay within the customary area, because the contract makes the action obligatory, and obligatory actions do not incur guarantee. However, if they exceed the customary zone, they guarantee the excess. If the patient dies, the practitioner pays half the blood-money (diyah) because the death was due to a mixed cause (permitted and non-permitted). The passage ends with a rare case of a circumciser cutting the glans: half diyah is required, and rules about qisas for partial cutting.

Key Terms
دن (dann): A large earthenware jar used for storing liquids.
خيار (khiyār): The option or choice given to the porter to determine whether his transgression was from the start or only at the break.
مضمون (maḍmūn): Guaranteed; the object that is subject to guarantee (daman) by the person in possession.
امين (amīn): Trustee; one who holds property by permission and is not liable for loss unless negligent or transgressive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A porter carrying a jar trips on a loose stone and breaks it. According to the text, which of the following is correct?
A) He guarantees the value and loses all wage.
B) He guarantees the value and receives wage proportionally.
C) He has a choice to declare transgression from start (no wage) or only at break (proportional wage).
D) He does not guarantee the value and receives full wage.

Key Takeaway
A porter's liability for a broken jar depends on the cause of break and gives him a choice that affects wage, with notable intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf for external causes.

Liability of Medical Practitioners (Cupper, Phlebotomist, Leecher)
Rulings on liability of medical practitioners: no liability within customary area, proportional liability for excess, half diyah for death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on liability for medical practitioners (cupper, phlebotomist, leecher) and identify when they are liable (exceeding customary bounds) and the consequences for harm or death.

Core Idea
A medical practitioner is not liable for harm within the customary area because the act is obligatory; exceeding it triggers proportional liability, including half diyah for death.

This lesson addresses the liability of medical practitioners such as cuppers, phlebotomists, and leechers. The Hanafi school holds that they are not liable for harm if they stay within the customary area of the procedure, because the act is obligatory (wajib) under the contract. However, if they exceed the customary bounds, they guarantee the excess, and if the patient dies, they pay half the blood-money (diyah) due to mixed causation. A rare case of circumciser cutting the glans illustrates proportional liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the practitioners covered
The text specifies three types: hajjam (cupper), fassad (phlebotomist), and bazzagh (leecher). All are considered medical practitioners whose work involves incisions or bloodletting.
The ruling may extend to other similar professions by qiyas.
2
Establish the default rule: no liability within customary area
If the practitioner does not exceed the customary site (al-mawdi' al-mu'tad) for the procedure, he does not guarantee any harm. The reasoning: the action is obligatory (wajib) by contract, and an obligatory action does not incur guarantee. Analogy: a judge who imposes a fixed punishment (hadd) or discretionary punishment (ta'zir) does not guarantee if the accused dies from it.
This is a key principle: wajib acts do not coexist with daman (guarantee).
3
Identify the exception: exceeding customary area
If the practitioner goes beyond the customary location (e.g., cuts deeper or in wrong place), he guarantees the harm caused by that excess. The guarantee is proportional: he is liable only for the damage resulting from the transgression.
The text uses 'yadman al-za'id' (he guarantees the excess).
4
Apply the ruling if the patient dies
If the patient dies as a result of the procedure, and the practitioner was within the customary area, he pays half the diyah (blood-money) of the person. This is because death occurred from a mixture of permitted (customary) and non-permitted (the practitioner's action) causes. The diyah is divided proportionally: half for the part that is not tortious.
This is a specific rule: half diyah, not full diyah.
5
Note the rare case of the circumciser cutting the glans
The text presents an unusual scenario: if a circumciser (khattan) cuts the glans, which is a complete organ, he must pay half the diyah (half of full diyah). This is considered rare because the maximum liability (half diyah) is owed for healing (i.e., injury) while a smaller amount would be owed for death (since death triggers half diyah as well). The lesson: the same half diyah applies both for injury to a complete organ and for death in mixed-causation cases.
This shows the complexity of proportional liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
حجام (hajjām): Cupper, one who performs hijama (cupping therapy).
فصاد (faṣṣād): Phlebotomist, one who performs bloodletting (fasd).
بزاغ (bazzāgh): One who applies leeches for bloodletting.
الموضع المعتاد (al-mawḍi' al-mu'tād): The customary or standard bodily location for the procedure.
دية النفس (diyat al-nafs): Blood-money for killing a person (full diyah).

A cupper performs hijama on the patient's neck (which is not the customary site) and the patient dies. According to the passage, what is the cupper's liability?
A) He is not liable because he had a contract.

C) He is liable for half diyah.
D) He is liable only for the excess harm, but since death occurred, it is considered excess, so half diyah.

Key Takeaway
Medical practitioners are not liable for harm within the customary area, but exceeding it incurs proportional liability, including half diyah for death.

Hired Worker Not Liable for Humans
Commentary on the matn ruling that a hired worker is not liable for humans who drown or fall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a hired worker is not liable for harm to humans under his care and explain the rational proof distinguishing contract liability from tort liability.

Core Idea
Humans are not guaranteed by contract; liability only arises from direct injury.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling in the chapter of hire (ijarah): when a hired worker causes accidental harm to a human being, is he liable for compensation? The answer is no—unless the harm was directly inflicted. The commentary explains the legal reasoning behind this distinction and addresses a difference of opinion within the school concerning children and adults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
What specific scenario does the matn describe? Identify the acts (drowning in a ship, falling from an animal) and the ruling: the hired worker is not liable for the human harmed.
The ruling applies to any hired worker, e.g., a ship captain or camel driver.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The commentary gives a rational proof (dalil aqli): 'because the human is not guaranteed by contract; rather, he is guaranteed by injury.' Understand the distinction between daman bil-'aqd (liability by contract) and daman bil-jinayah (liability by tort).
This is a general principle, not a specific text from Quran or Sunnah.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A reported opinion (qil) differentiates between an adult who can manage himself and a child who cannot, treating the child like a chattel. Note the two positions and the author's resolution.
The author declares the correct view (sahih) that there is no difference.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
State the operative ruling for fatwa: the hired worker is not liable for any human, regardless of age, unless he causes direct injury (jinaayah).
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (ولا يضمن به بني آدم ممن غرق في السفينة أو سقط من الدابة ولو كان بسوقه وقوده لأن الآدمي لا يضمن بالعقد وإنما يضمن بالجناية وقيل هذا إذا كان كبيراً ممن يستمسك بنفسه ويركب وحده وإلا فهو كالمتاع والصحيح أنه لا فرق .
May Allah have mercy on him: (And he is not liable for a human being, from among those who drown in the ship or fall from the animal, even if by his driving and leading, because the human is not guaranteed by contract; rather, he is guaranteed by injury. And it was said: This is if he is an adult who can hold himself and rides alone; otherwise, he is like a chattel. And the correct position is that there is no difference.)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling that a hired worker is not liable for harm befalling a human being under his charge, such as a passenger drowning when the ship sinks or a rider falling from the animal, even if the worker caused the fall by driving the animal. The reason given is that humans are not guaranteed by a contract of hire (i.e., the hired worker does not assume liability for the human's safety by contract); liability arises only from direct injury (jinaayah). There is a reported opinion that this applies only to an adult who can hold himself and ride alone; for a child or incapable person, it would be like a chattel (so the hired worker would be liable). However, the correct position (as stated) is that there is no difference between adult and child—the hired worker is not liable in either case.

Key Terms
بني آدم (bani adam): Human beings as opposed to inanimate objects or animals.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
جناية (jinaayah): Direct injury or tort, which is a basis for liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is a hired camel driver liable if a passenger falls from the camel and dies?
A) Yes, because he was responsible for the passenger's safety.
B) Yes, because the passenger is like a chattel.
C) No, because humans are not guaranteed by contract.
D) No, only if the driver intentionally pushed him.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a hired worker is not liable for harm to human beings under his care unless caused by direct injury (jinaayah).

Jar Broken in Transit: Liability and the Khiyar Option
Commentary on the matn ruling about a jar breaking in the road, detailing liability and khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on porter liability when a jar breaks in transit, explain the owner's khiyar option, and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
When a jar breaks by the porter's act, the owner chooses: treat as trespass from start (full liability, no wage) or only at break (partial liability, wage).

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a common fiqh scenario: a hired porter carries a jar that breaks in the road. The ruling hinges on whether the break was caused by the porter's action or an external force. The owner is given a legal option (khiyar) that significantly affects the financial consequences. We will analyse the ruling, the proofs, and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The jar breaks in the road. The porter is liable if it was destroyed by his act. The basis: what is included in the contract is non-corrupt work; corruption is not contracted, so liability arises.
The ruling applies when the porter's action directly causes the break.
2
Understand the khiyar (option)
The owner can choose between two interpretations: either the porter was a trespasser from the start, or he was initially permitted and trespass occurred only at the moment of break. Each choice leads to different liability and wage consequences.
The khiyar exists because there is uncertainty about the nature of the porter's action.
3
Examine the external cause scenario
If the break occurs due to something beyond the porter's control (e.g., jostling by crowd), Imam Abu Hanifa holds no liability and no wage, while the two companions hold liability (no khiyar because the chattel is guaranteed).
The disagreement is based on whether the chattel is considered guaranteed (madmin) under the contract.
4
Note the 'in the road' restriction
The ruling applies only while the jar is in transit. If it breaks after reaching the porter's home, according to Muhammad's later opinion, there is no liability and the porter gets full wage. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's earlier opinion impose liability.
This shows the evolution of Muhammad al-Shaybani's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن انكسر دن في الطريق ضمن الحمال قيمته في محله ولا أجر أوفى موضع الانكسار وأجره بحسابه أما الضمان فلأنه تلف بفعله لأن الداخل تحت العقد عمل غير مفسد والمفسد غير داخل فيضمن على ما بينا وأما الخيار فلأنه إذا انكسر في الطريق شيء واحد تبين أنه وقع تعدياً هذه الوجه، وله وجه آخر وهو الحمل حصل بأمره فلم يكن متعدياً وإنما صار تعدياً عند الكسر فيميل إلى أي الجهتين شاء، فإن مال إلى كونه متعدياً من الابتداء ضمنه قيمته ولا أجر له وإن مال إلى كونه مأذوناً فيه في الابتداء وإنما حصل التعدي عند الكسر ضمنه قيمته في موضع الكسر وأعطاه الأجر بحسابه. قال في شرح الطحاوي: معناه أسقط قدر الأجرة هذا إذا انكسر بصنعه بأن زلق وعثر، فإن عثر بغير صنعه بأن زحمه الناس لا يضمن عند الإمام ولا أجر له، وعندهما يضمن قيمته في موضع ما انكسر ولا يخير لأن العين مضمونة عندهما على ما بينا. قال في التتارخانية : هذا إذا انكسر الدن بجناية يده أما إذا حصل لا بجناية يده؛ فإن كان يأمر لا يمكن التحرز عنه لا ضمان عليه بالإجماع، وإن هلك بأمر يمكن التحرز عنه فكذلك عند الإمام، وعندهما يجب الضمان وللمالك الخيار. وقوله في الطريق قال في الذخيرة. قيد احترازي فإذا انكسر الدن بعد ما انتهى به إلى بيته فله الأجر ولا ضمان عليه وهذا قول محمد أخراً، أما على قول أبي يوسف وهو قول محمد أولاً يجب أن يكون ضامناً !
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if a jar breaks in the road, the porter is liable for its value at its location, and there is no wage for the portion beyond the break, and his wage is calculated proportionally. As for the liability, it is because it was destroyed by his act; because what is included under the contract is work that does not corrupt, and what corrupts is not included, so he is liable as we have explained. As for the option, it is because when it breaks in the road, it becomes clear that it occurred as a trespass in this respect, and there is another respect: the carrying occurred by his order, so he was not a trespasser, but he became a trespasser at the time of breaking. So he may incline to whichever of the two aspects he wishes. If he inclines to it being a trespass from the beginning, he is liable for its full value and has no wage. If he inclines to it being permitted from the beginning and the trespass only occurred at the break, he is liable for its value at the location of the break and is given his wage proportionally. It is said in Sharh al-Tahawi: Its meaning is that he drops the amount of the wage. This is if it broke through his action, such as by slipping and stumbling. If it stumbled without his action, such as from being jostled by people, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa he is not liable and has no wage; according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is liable for its value at the location of the break and there is no option, because according to them the chattel is guaranteed as we have stated. It is said in al-Tatar Khaniyya: This is if the jar broke through a tort of his hand; if it occurred without a tort of his hand, then if it was from something which cannot be avoided, there is no liability by consensus. If it perished through something which can be avoided, then similarly according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and according to the two companions liability is due and the owner has the option. His statement 'in the road' — it is said in al-Dhakhira: a precautionary restriction. So if the jar broke after he had reached his house, then he has the wage and no liability upon him. This is the latter opinion of Muhammad. As for the opinion of Abu Yusuf, which was also the earlier opinion of Muhammad, he would be liable.)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of a jar being transported by a hired porter that breaks in the road. The porter is liable for the value of the jar because he caused its destruction. However, the owner has an option (khiyar): he can either treat the porter as having been a trespasser from the start, in which case the porter owes full value and no wage; or treat the porter as having been authorized initially, with the trespass occurring only at the moment of breakage, in which case the porter owes the value at the break location and receives his wage proportionally. The passage also provides details from Sharh al-Tahawi, distinguishing between breakage caused by the porter's own act (e.g., slipping) versus external causes (e.g., being jostled by people). In the latter case, Imam Abu Hanifa holds no liability, while the two companions hold liability. From al-Tatar Khaniyya, the discussion includes cases where the breakage is not by a direct tort, with different rulings as mentioned. Finally, the restriction 'in the road' is noted: if the jar breaks after reaching the porter's own home, then according to Muhammad's later opinion, the porter is not liable and receives his full wage.

Key Terms
دن (dunn): A jar or container, typically for liquids, that is being transported.
خيار (khiyar): The option given to the owner to choose between two legal consequences: treating the porter as trespasser from start or only at break.
تعدي (ta'addi): Trespass or transgression beyond what is permitted in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a jar breaking in the road, if the owner chooses to treat the porter as a trespasser from the beginning, what is the consequence according to the Hanafi school?
A) The porter pays full value and receives no wage.
B) The porter pays the value at the break location and receives half wage.
C) The porter pays nothing because it was an accident.
D) The porter must pay full value but can keep the wage.

Key Takeaway
The owner's khiyar in jar-transport cases allows him to choose between full liability with no wage or partial liability with proportional wage, based on the timing of the trespass.
