The Halwani-Sarakhsi Dispute on Travel with a Slave in Sulh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of sulh rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings on traveling with a slave in a sulh agreement versus an ijarah contract according to the Hanafi school, and evaluate the reasoning of Imam al-Halwani and Imam al-Sarakhsi.

Core Idea
In sulh, the right to service may permit travel; in ijarah, it does not.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two great Hanafi scholars, al-Halwani and al-Sarakhsi. The context is a sulh (settlement) agreement where one party receives the service of a slave for a year. The question is whether the recipient can travel with the slave. Understanding this difference reveals deeper principles about the nature of sulh compared to ijarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the specific legal question being debated? Who are the two scholars and what are their positions?
The case: a claimant settles a house claim for the service of a slave for one year.
2
State al-Halwani's position
Al-Halwani restricts 'taking out to his family' to local movement, not travel. What is his likely reasoning?
He interprets the phrase narrowly to avoid extra burden on the slave owner.
3
State al-Sarakhsi's position
Al-Sarakhsi allows travel in sulh but not in ijarah. What distinction is he making?
He views sulh as granting broader rights than ijarah.
4
Seek the difference
The text ends by asking for the difference. Reflect: What makes sulh different from ijarah such that travel is permitted in one but not the other?
Key distinction: sulh is a compromise ending a dispute, often transferring ownership of the right more broadly; ijarah is a precise hire contract with defined limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكره في صلح الأصل: ادعى على آخر داراً فصالحه على خدمة عبده سنة كان له أن يخرج بالعبد إلى أهله قال شمس الأمة الحلواني : لم يرد بإخراجه إلى أهله السفر وإنما أراد القرية وأفنية المصر. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : له في مسألة الصلح أن يسافر بخلاف مسألة الإجارة اهـ. ويطلب الفرق .
And it is mentioned in Sulh al-Asl: He claimed a house from another, and he settled with him on the service of his slave for a year; he has the right to take the slave out to his family. Shams al-Umma al-Halwani said: He did not mean by 'taking out to his family' travel; rather he meant the village and outskirts of the city. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: In the matter of sulh, he may travel, unlike the matter of ijarah. End. And the difference is sought.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two great Hanafi scholars. The context is a sulh (settlement) agreement where one person claims a house from another, and they settle by the defendant providing the service of his slave for a year. The question is whether the claimant can take the slave out to his family (i.e., to a different location). Al-Halwani says the permission to take the slave to his family does not mean travel to a distant place; it only means within the village or outskirts. Al-Sarakhsi, on the other hand, says that in the case of sulh, the claimant can travel with the slave, unlike in a regular ijarah contract where travel is restricted. The text concludes by saying the difference is sought, implying that the reader should ponder the distinction between the two contracts.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): a settlement or compromise in a dispute
إجارة (ijarah): a contract of hire or lease of services or usufruct
خدمة (khidma): service or work performed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Sarakhsi, can the claimant in a sulh agreement travel with the slave?
A) No, because it is like ijarah.
B) Yes, unlike ijarah.
C) No, because the slave is not his.
D) Only with the owner's permission.

Key Takeaway
The Halwani-Sarakhsi dispute illustrates that the rights in a sulh agreement may be broader than in an ijarah contract, particularly regarding travel with the subject of service.

Istihsan Overriding Qiyas in Hiring a Mahjur Slave
Explaining istihsan in hiring a mahjur slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi istihsan prevents the hirer from recovering wages paid to a mahjur slave, and apply this reasoning to similar sub-cases.

Core Idea
Istihsan preserves benefit: the hirer cannot take back wages from a mahjur slave whose work was beneficial.

In this lesson, we explore a key application of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh: hiring a slave under interdiction (mahjur) for work. According to strict qiyas, the contract is invalid, so the wage should remain the hirer's property. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan to uphold the beneficial nature of the slave's labor, preventing the hirer from recovering the wage. We will unpack the reasoning and examine related sub-rulings where different outcomes arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn ruling
The author says: 'And the hirer does not take from a slave who is under interdiction a wage that he paid for his work.' This is the core ruling. Recognize that the hirer loses the right to recovery.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq; the commentary expands on it.
2
Recognize the qiyas argument
The comment explains qiyas: since the mahjur's contract is invalid, the wage remains the hirer's property. The hirer is a usurper, so he can reclaim the wage. This is the default analogy.
Qiyas jaliy would allow recovery.
3
Understand the istihsan rebuttal
Istihsan overrides qiyas because the slave's action is beneficial on the assumption of safety (i.e., the work is done safely). The slave owns the wage because he is permitted to benefit from his labor; otherwise, the master's benefit would be lost.
The key phrase: 'beneficial on the assumption of safety' (نافع على تقدير السلامة).
4
Apply to specific sub-cases
The text lists variations: if the slave perishes during use, the hirer must pay his value (no wage recovery). If the master frees the slave mid-term, the wage for past period goes to master, future to slave. If the slave himself hires out (minor) and works, his wage is his own.
These sub-cases show the boundaries of the istihsan ruling.
5
Note intra-Hanafi differences
In sub-rulings like mukatib's slave lease or istihqaq (claimed slave), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ. For example, upon mukatib's inability, Abu Yusuf keeps the lease valid; Muhammad says it is rescinded.
These differences affect fatwa in specific scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يأخذ المستأجر من عبد محجور عليه أجراً دفعه لعمله) يعني لو استأجر رجل عبداً محجوراً عليه من نفسه فعمل وأعطاه الأجر ليس للمستأجر أن يأخذ منه، والقياس له أن يأخذه منه لأن عقد المحجور عليه لا يجوز فيبقى على ملك المستأجر لأنه بالاستعمال صار غاصباً له ولهذا يجب عليه ضمان قيمته إذا هلك ومنافع المغصوب لا تضمن عندنا فيبقى المدفوع على ملكه فله أن يسترد لأن الاستحسان لا قياساً. وفي التصرف من العبد في هذه الحالة نافع على تقدير السلامة صار على تقدير الهلاك والنافع مأذون فيه فيملكه العبد فيخرج الأجر عن ملكه فبعد ما سلم تمحض نفعاً في حق المولى لأنه إذا جاز تحصل للمولى الأجر ولو لم يجز ضاعت منافع العبد فتعين القول بالجواز وصح قبض العبد الأجرة فلا يسترد بخلاف ما إذا هلك العبد في حالة الاستعمال فإنه يجب على المستأجر قيمته، وإذا صار غاصباً من وقت الاستعمال فيصير مستوفياً منفعة عبد نفسه فلا يجب عليه الأجرة للصبي المحجور عليه إذا استأجر نفسه وسلم فإن الأجرة له لأنه غير ممنوع عما ينفعه وفي النهاية الأجر الذي يجب في هاتين الصورتين هو أجر المثل فإن أعتقه المولى في نصف المدة نفذت الإجارة ولا خيار للعبد وأجر ما مضى للمولى والأجرة في المستقبل للعبد وفي قاضيخان الأب والجد ووصيهما إذا أجر عبداً لصبي سنين ثم بلغ الغلام لم يكن له أن يفسخ والصبي إذا أجر نفسه وسلم ثم بلغ له أن يفسخ الإجارة وفي المحيط : المكاتب إذا أجر عبده ثم عجز المكاتب رد في الرق فالإجارة باقية في قول أبي يوسف وقال محمد: تنتقض اهـ. وفي التتارخانية: ولو أجر الرجل عبداً ثم استحق وأجاز المستحق الإجارة فإن كانت الإجارة قبل استيفاء المنفعة جاز وكانت الإجرة للمالك، وإن أجاز بعد استيفاء المنفعة لم تعتبر الإجارة ولا أجر للعاقد، وإن أجاز في بعض المدة فالماضي له والباقي للمالك عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: أجرة ما مضى للغاصب وما بقي فهو للمالك ا هـ .
He (the author) said: 'And the hirer does not take from a slave who is under interdiction a wage that he paid for his work.' Meaning: if a man hires a slave who is under interdiction from himself, and he works and gives him the wage, the hirer may not take it from him. The qiyas is that he may take it from him because the contract of the mahjur is not valid, so it remains in the ownership of the hirer because by using him he became a usurper, and for this reason he must guarantee his value if he perishes, and the usufruct of a usurped item is not guaranteed with us, so the payment remains his property, so he may take it back. But the istihsan (preference) is not by qiyas. And the disposition of the slave in this case is beneficial on the assumption of safety, but on the assumption of destruction, and the beneficial is permitted, so the slave owns it, and the wage leaves his ownership. So after safety, it becomes purely beneficial for the master, because if it is permitted, the master obtains the wage, and if it were not permitted, the benefits of the slave would be wasted, so permissibility is determined. And the slave's taking of the wage is valid, so he does not take it back, unlike if the slave perishes during use, then the hirer must pay its value. And when he becomes a usurper from the time of use, he becomes a user of the usufruct of his own slave, so no wage is due upon him. For the minor under interdiction, if he hires himself and works, the wage is his because he is not prevented from what benefits him. In al-Nihaya, the wage due in these two cases is the fair wage. If the master frees him in the middle of the term, the lease is effective and the slave has no option, and the wage for the past is for the master, and for the future for the slave. In Qadi Khan: The father and grandfather and their executor, if they lease the slave of a boy for years, then the boy attains majority, he has no right to rescind. And the boy, if he hires himself and works and then attains majority, he has the right to rescind the lease. In al-Muhit: The mukatib, if he leases his slave and then the mukatib becomes incapable and is returned to slavery, the lease remains according to Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad says it is rescinded. End. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If a man leases a slave and then the slave is claimed (by someone else), and the claimant ratifies the lease, then if the ratification is before the usufruct is consumed, the lease is valid and the wage is for the owner; if after consumption, the lease is not considered and no wage for the contractor; if he ratifies a part of the term, then the past wage is for the claimant and the remaining for the owner according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad says: the wage for the past is for the usurper and the remaining for the owner. End.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a slave who is under interdiction (mahjur alayh) due to minority or insolvency. The matn states that the hirer (who hired such a slave) cannot take back the wage he paid to the slave. The commentary explains the difference between qiyas and istihsan. According to qiyas (analogy), the contract of a mahjur is invalid, so the wage remains the hirer's property, and since the hirer becomes a usurper by using the slave, he should be able to recover the wage. However, istihsan (juristic preference) dictates otherwise: the slave's action is beneficial on the assumption of safety (i.e., the slave works and the master benefits), so the slave owns the wage; otherwise the slave's labor would be wasted. The commentary also clarifies that in different cases (e.g., if the slave perishes, if the master frees him mid-term, if the slave is a minor, if the slave is a mukatib, or if the slave is claimed by a third party), the rulings vary. Many of these sub-rulings involve disagreements between Abu Hanifa's students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (mahjur alayhi): a person under legal interdiction due to minority or insanity
استحسان (istihsan): juristic preference overriding qiyas
قياس (qiyas): analogical reasoning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the istihsan ruling in the text, the hirer cannot recover the wage because:
A) the slave's contract is valid.
B) the slave's work is beneficial and he owns the wage.
C) the master is not liable.
D) the hirer must prove identity.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan in Hanafi fiqh allows overriding qiyas to preserve benefit (maslaha); thus the hirer cannot recover wages paid to a mahjur slave for beneficial work.

Ruling: Usurper Not Liable for Wage Taken from Hired Slave
Matn ruling with brief commentary beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a usurper of a slave does not guarantee the wage he takes from the slave and explain the legal rationale.

Core Idea
The usurper guarantees the slave but not the wage — the wage is the slave's own property.

The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states a specific rule: when a person usurps a slave who has hired himself out, the usurper does not have to compensate for the wage he takes from the slave's hand. At first glance, this seems to contradict the general strict liability of a usurper. Hanafi jurists, however, draw a distinction between the slave's corporeal property and his earnings. In this lesson, we examine the ruling and its underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a usurper (ghāṣib) taking a wage from a hired-out slave. Ruling: the usurper does not guarantee (ḍamān) that wage to the owner.
The Arabic phrase is 'lā yaḍmanu' – he does not guarantee.
2
Distinguish the corpus from earnings
The usurper is liable for the slave itself (the 'ayn), but the wage is considered the slave's own earning (if the slave had permission to trade). The wage is not part of the slave's corpus; it is a separate right owned by the slave.
The slave's right to own earnings is established in Hanafi law for certain categories of slaves (e.g., mukātab or with owner's permission).
3
Identify the legal consequence
Therefore, the usurper's taking of the wage is a transgression against the slave's property, not against the owner. The owner cannot claim compensation for that wage from the usurper.
The usurper may still be liable to the slave, but that is a separate matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولا يضمن غاصب العبد ما أكل من أجره) معناه إذا غصب رجل عبداً أجر العبد نفسه فأخذ الغاصب من يد العبد الأجرة
He (the author) said: 'And the usurper of the slave does not guarantee what he ate of his wage.' Its meaning: if a person usurps a slave, and the slave hires himself out, and the usurper takes the wage from the hand of the slave.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a brief ruling from the commentary. The matn states that when a person usurps a slave (i.e., takes him illegally), and then the slave hires himself out to another person and receives a wage, if the usurper then takes that wage from the slave, the usurper is not liable to compensate (damān) for that wage. The explanation begins but is cut off in the available pages. The rationale, typically, is that the slave is the owner of his own earnings (if he is a mukatib or has permission), and the usurper's taking of the wage is a wrongful act but the wage itself is the property of the slave; however, the usurper's liability is not established because the wage is a debt? Or other reasons. The full reasoning would be found in the complete text.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): usurper, one who takes property without right
ضمان (ḍamān): guarantee, liability to compensate

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the usurper is responsible for all losses.
B) No, the usurper does not guarantee that wage.
C) Yes, if the wage exceeds the slave's market value.
D) Only if the slave demands it.

Key Takeaway
The usurper's liability for the slave itself does not extend to the slave's earnings that the usurper takes.

Wage of a Mahjur Slave: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Hashiyah-style commentary on the wage of a mahjur slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a hirer cannot reclaim wages paid to a mahjur slave, and distinguish between the qiyas and istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas when the slave's action is beneficial, granting him ownership of his wage.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we encounter a classic Hanafi example of istihsan overriding qiyas. A mahjur slave (interdicted due to minority or insanity) hires himself out and receives wages. By strict qiyas, the contract is invalid and the wage should remain the hirer's. But istihsan, based on the benefit (nafi') to the master, validates the slave's receipt. This lesson walks through the reasoning and explores its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the legal outcome regarding the wage of a mahjur slave who has been hired and paid.
The ruling is that the hirer cannot reclaim the wage he paid to the slave.
2
State the qiyas argument
Explain by pure analogy (qiyas) why the hirer should be able to reclaim the wage.
The contract of a mahjur person is invalid, so the wage remains the hirer's property because the slave is a usurper of his own services, and the benefits of usurped property are not held liable.
3
Recognize the istihsan argument
Identify on what basis the Hanafi school overrides qiyas in this case.
Istihsan (juristic preference) considers the transaction beneficial (nafi') to the master, so the slave is permitted to receive and own the wage.
4
Analyze the benefit (nafi') reasoning
Break down how the transaction is beneficial on both assumptions of safety or destruction of the slave.
If the slave survives, the master gets the wage; if the slave perishes, the hirer must pay his value. In both outcomes, the master benefits.
5
Apply the ruling to derivative cases
Consider related scenarios from the passage, such as when the slave is freed mid-term, or the hirer is a minor.
The wage is split between master and slave proportionally; a minor hirer cannot rescind if the contract was made by his guardian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يأخذ المستأجر من عبد محجور عليه أجراً دفعه لعمله) يعني لو استأجر رجل عبداً محجوراً عليه من نفسه فعمل وأعطاه الأجر ليس للمستأجر أن يأخذ منه، والقياس له أن يأخذه منه لأن عقد المحجور عليه لا يجوز فيبقى على ملك المستأجر لأنه بالاستعمال صار غاصباً له ولهذا يجب عليه ضمان قيمته إذا هلك ومنافع المغصوب لا تضمن عندنا فيبقى المدفوع على ملكه فله أن يسترد لأن الاستحسان لا قياساً. وفي التصرف من العبد في هذه الحالة نافع على تقدير السلامة صار على تقدير الهلاك والنافع مأذون فيه فيملكه العبد فيخرج الأجر عن ملكه فبعد ما سلم تمحض نفعاً في حق المولى لأنه إذا جاز تحصل للمولى الأجر ولو لم يجز ضاعت منافع العبد فتعين القول بالجواز وصح قبض العبد الأجرة فلا يسترد بخلاف ما إذا هلك العبد في حالة الاستعمال فإنه يجب على المستأجر قيمته، وإذا صار غاصباً من وقت الاستعمال فيصير مستوفياً منفعة عبد نفسه فلا يجب عليه الأجرة للصبي المحجور عليه إذا استأجر نفسه وسلم فإن الأجرة له لأنه غير ممنوع عما ينفعه وفي النهاية الأجر الذي يجب في هاتين الصورتين هو أجر المثل فإن أعتقه المولى في نصف المدة نفذت الإجارة ولا خيار للعبد وأجر ما مضى للمولى والأجرة في المستقبل للعبد وفي قاضيخان الأب والجد ووصيهما إذا أجر عبداً لصبي سنين ثم بلغ الغلام لم يكن له أن يفسخ والصبي إذا أجر نفسه وسلم ثم بلغ له أن يفسخ الإجارة وفي المحيط : المكاتب إذا أجر عبده ثم عجز المكاتب رد في الرق فالإجارة باقية في قول أبي يوسف وقال محمد: تنتقض اهـ. وفي التتارخانية: ولو أجر الرجل عبداً ثم استحق وأجاز المستحق الإجارة فإن كانت الإجارة قبل استيفاء المنفعة جاز وكانت الإجرة للمالك، وإن أجاز بعد استيفاء المنفعة لم تعتبر الإجارة ولا أجر للعاقد، وإن أجاز في بعض المدة فالماضي له والباقي للمالك عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: أجرة ما مضى للغاصب وما بقي فهو للمالك ا هـ .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the hirer does not take from a slave who is under interdiction a wage that he paid for his work) meaning: if a man hires a slave who is under interdiction from himself, and he works and gives him the wage, it is not permissible for the hirer to take it from him. By analogy (qiyas), he should be able to take it from him because the contract of the one under interdiction is not valid, so it remains in the ownership of the hirer because by using him he becomes a usurper (ghasib) of him, and for this reason he is liable for his value if he perishes, and the benefits of the usurped property are not liable according to us, so the payment remains in his ownership, so he can reclaim it. But istihsan [overrules] not qiyas. And the disposition of the slave in this situation is beneficial on the assumption of safety, and it becomes [beneficial] on the assumption of destruction, and the beneficial act is permitted, so the slave owns it, and the wage leaves his ownership. After it is safe, it becomes purely beneficial for the master because if it is allowed, the master receives the wage, and if not, the benefits of the slave are wasted. So the ruling of permissibility is established, and the slave's receipt of the wage is valid, so he cannot reclaim it. Unlike the case where the slave perishes during use, then the hirer must pay his value. And when he becomes a usurper from the time of use, he becomes a recipient of the benefit of his own slave, so the wage is not obligatory to the minor under interdiction. If he hires himself and delivers, the wage is his because he is not prevented from what benefits him. And in al-Nihayah: The wage due in these two cases is the fair wage (ujr al-mithl). If the master frees him mid-term, the hire is executed without an option for the slave, and the past wage is for the master and the future wage for the slave. In Qadikhan: The father, grandfather, and their trustees, if they hire a slave for a boy for years, then the boy reaches puberty, he cannot rescind; but if the boy hires himself and delivers, then reaches puberty, he can rescind the hire. In al-Muhit: The slave who has made a kitabah (mukatib) hires out his slave, then the mukatib becomes unable, he reverts to slavery; the hire remains valid according to Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad says it is rescinded. In al-Tatarhaniyyah: If a man hires out a slave, then [the slave] is claimed by someone, and the claimant approves the hire: if the hire was before the benefit was received, it is valid and the wage is for the owner; if he approves after the benefit was received, the hire is not considered and there is no wage for the contractor; if he approves in part of the period, then the past is for [the contractor] and the remaining for the owner according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: the wage for the past is for the usurper and what remains is for the owner.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The ruling: If a person hires a slave who is under interdiction (mahjur 'alayh) because of minority or insanity, and the slave works and receives wages, the hirer cannot take back those wages. The author explains that by qiyas (analogy), the hirer could take back the wage because the contract of a mahjur is invalid, so the wage should remain the hirer's property; but istihsan (juristic preference) prevails. The istihsan reasoning: The slave's action of hiring himself out is beneficial (nafi') because if it turns out well, the master gets the wage; if the slave perishes, the compensation (value) is paid. Since it is beneficial, the slave is considered permitted to do it, so he owns the wage. Hence the hirer cannot reclaim it. However, if the slave perishes during the work, the hirer must pay his value (as a usurper). The commentary then follows with derivative rulings: if a minor hires himself and delivers, the wage is his; the wage in such cases is ujr al-mithl; if the master frees the slave mid-term, the hire continues and the wage is split between master and slave proportionally; various intra-Hanafi differences are noted regarding the validity of the hire when the slave is a mukatib, etc. The authority weight: This is from the later fatawa and commentaries, but the principle is from Zahir al-Riwayah as the matn indicates.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayh): A person who is interdicted from managing his own financial affairs, such as a minor or an insane person.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger indication from the sources or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that the hirer cannot reclaim wages paid to a mahjur slave, despite the contract being invalid by qiyas?
A) Because the slave is considered a usurper and cannot own property.
B) Because istihsan considers the transaction beneficial, so the slave is permitted to receive and own the wage.
C) Because the hirer is considered a guarantor of the slave's work.
D) Because the master is the true owner of all slave earnings.

Key Takeaway
A mahjur slave's receipt of wage is valid and cannot be reclaimed by the hirer because istihsan recognizes the beneficial nature of the transaction.

Rulings on Hiring Close Relatives for Service (Khidma)
Matn rulings on hiring close relatives for service with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on hiring one's father, mother, grandmother, uncle, and brother for personal service and distinguish between valid and invalid contracts.

Core Idea
Hiring a close relative for personal service is invalid due to disrespect, but specific tasks like herding are permissible.

The Hanafi school provides clear rules for hiring close relatives. The key distinction is between personal service (khidma), which implies subordination, and specific tasks. This lesson presents the matn rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq along with a related fatwa on hiring a Muslim to a disbeliever and a discussion on sulh versus ijarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative and type of service
Determine which relative is being hired and whether the work is personal service (khidma) or a specific task like herding.
The text differentiates between service and specific tasks.
2
State the ruling for each case
Use the matn to determine validity and wage entitlement. For mother/ grandmother, note the exception regarding wages.
The ruling may vary by relative and task.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
The prohibition is rooted in respect (hurmat) for parents and older relatives. This principle applies regardless of religion.
No difference between Muslim and non-Muslim in the father case.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
Use the distinction between khidma and specific tasks to evaluate scenarios not explicitly mentioned.
For example, hiring a father to teach is likely valid.
5
Distinguish ijarah from sulh
The passage ends with a difference of opinion regarding travel in sulh contracts. Note that sulh offers flexibility that ijarah does not.
The difference is sought; both opinions are respected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر أباه للخدمة لا يجوز ولا أجر له لا فرق بين الكافر والمسلم. ولو استأجر أباه لرعي غنمه يجوز، ولو استأجر أمه أو جدته للخدمة لا يجوز، ولو خدم فله المسمى. ولو استأجر عمه وهو أكبر منه أو أخاه وهو أكبر منه لا يجوز. وفي فتاوى الفضلي : لا يجوز إجارة المسلم نفسه من كافر في الخدمة وفيما غير الخدمة يجوز. وذكره في صلح الأصل: ادعى على آخر داراً فصالحه على خدمة عبده سنة كان له أن يخرج بالعبد إلى أهله قال شمس الأمة الحلواني : لم يرد بإخراجه إلى أهله السفر وإنما أراد القرية وأفنية المصر. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : له في مسألة الصلح أن يسافر بخلاف مسألة الإجارة اهـ. ويطلب الفرق.
And if he hires his father for service, it is not permissible and he has no wage, no difference between disbeliever and Muslim. And if he hires his father to herd his sheep, it is permissible. And if he hires his mother or his grandmother for service, it is not permissible, and if she serves, she has the named wage. And if he hires his paternal uncle who is older than him or his brother who is older than him, it is not permissible. And in Fatawa al-Fadli: It is not permissible for a Muslim to hire himself out to a disbeliever in service, but in other than service it is permissible. And it is mentioned in Sulh al-Asl: If someone claims a house from another and settles with him on the service of his slave for a year, he has the right to take the slave out to his family. Shams al-Umma al-Halwani said: He did not mean by 'take out to his family' travel, but rather he meant the village and the outskirts of the city. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarkhasi said: In the case of sulh he may travel, unlike the case of ijarah. End. And the difference is sought.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a passage consisting of several matn rulings from the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, setting out rules for hiring certain relatives. The rulings are stated without extensive proof, but are based on the principle that hiring one's father or similar close relatives for personal service (khidma) is invalid because it implies a kind of subordination that is disrespectful. However, exceptions exist: hiring one's father to herd sheep is permissible because it is not personal service but a specific task. Similarly, hiring mother or grandmother is invalid for service, but if they serve, they are entitled to the agreed wage (not nil). Hiring an older uncle or older brother is also invalid. The passage also includes a fatwa from Fatawa al-Fadli: a Muslim may not hire himself to a kafir for service, but may for other work. Finally, there is a discussion from the chapter of sulh: in a settlement involving service of a slave, the slave may be taken out to the claimant's family; there is a difference of opinion between Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarkhasi on whether this allows travel. The passage ends by stating that the difference should be sought.

Key Terms
الخدمة (al-khidma): Personal service, such as attending, serving food, etc., which implies a degree of subordination.
صلح الأصل (Sulh al-Asl): A reference to the work Salh al-Asl by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the subject of settlement in claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Liability of a Usurper for Consumed Wage of a Usurped Slave
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a usurper's liability for consuming a usurped slave's wage, explain the condition of muhraz (protected property), and apply the internal school dispute (Imam vs. Sahibayn) to new scenarios.

Core Idea
A usurper is not liable for consuming the wage because it is not muhraz from him.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a nuanced case within the law of usurpation (ghasb). When a usurper takes control of a slave, and the slave earns a wage which the usurper then consumes, does the usurper owe compensation? Imam Abu Hanifa says no, while his two students (Sahibayn) say yes. The key lies in the concept of muhraz (legally protected property). Let us unpack the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and the dispute
Read the matn and commentary: the usurper of a slave is not liable for consuming the wage according to Imam. The Sahibayn hold him liable. Note that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The matn follows Imam's view, which is mufta bihi.
2
Understand Imam's concept of muhraz
Imam argues that liability requires destroying property that is muhraz (protected) and mutaqawwim. The wage in the slave's hand is not muhraz because the slave is under the usurper's control, so the usurper is not an outsider.
Contrast with property in the owner's hand or his agent's.
3
Analyze the analogies (qiyas)
Imam draws two analogies: (1) like stolen property after amputation – the thief is not liable for it because it is no longer considered protected from him; (2) the wage is a substitute for the benefit of the slave, and since the usurper is not liable for destroying the benefit (a general rule in ghasb), he is not liable for the substitute.
The second analogy uses the principle that the substitute follows the original (بدل حكمه حكم المبدل).
4
Consider the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn argue simply: the usurper destroyed property of another without permission, so he is liable. They do not accept the muhraz distinction in this context.
Their position is based on general liability for property destruction.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If the usurper consumes the wage, according to Imam he owes nothing; according to Sahibayn he must pay the owner. The practical difference is clear in any case where a usurper uses the slave's earnings.
This also affects cases like the slave's offspring, where the ruling is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولا يضمن غاصب العبد ما أكل من أجره) معناه إذا غصب رجل عبداً أجر العبد نفسه فأخذ الغاصب من يد العبد الأجرة وأكلها فلا ضمان عليه عند الإمام. وقالا عليه ضمانه لأنه أتلف مال الغير بغير إذنه ولا تأويل له. وللإمام أن الضمان إنما يجب بإتلاف مال محرز متقوم وهذا ليس بمحرز لأن الإحراز يكون بيده أو بيد نائبه وهذا ليس في يده ولا يد نائبه لأن الغاصب ليس بنائب عنه ولا العبد بل العبد وما في يده في يد الغاصب فلم يكن محرزاً فلا ضمان فصار نظير المال المسروق في يد السارق بعد القطع ولأن الأجرة بدل المنفعة والبدل حكمه حكم المبدل، ولو أتلف الغاصب المنفعة لا يضمن فكذا بدلها وما تردد بين أصلين توفر فيه حظهما فرجحنا جانب المالك عند بقاء الأجر في يده فقلنا المالك أحق به، ورجحنا جانب الغاصب في حق الضمان وقلنا لا ضمان عليه إذا أكل الأجرة بخلاف ولد المغصوب حيث يجب على الغاصب ضمانه بالإتلاف تعدياً لأنه ليس ببدل المنفعة بل هو جزء الأم فيضمنه بالتعدي كالأم، ولهذا لو استولدها الغاصب لا يكون الولد له ولو أجر العبد كان الأجر له .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the usurper of the slave is not liable for what he consumes of his wage) meaning: if a man usurps a slave, and the slave hires himself out, and the usurper takes the wage from the slave's hand and consumes it, there is no liability upon him according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. But the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] say he is liable for it because he destroyed the property of another without his permission and he has no legal justification. According to Imam, liability is only incurred by destroying property that is protected (muhraz) and valuable (mutaqawwim), and this [wage] is not protected because protection is by one's own hand or the hand of one's deputy, and this [wage] is not in his hand nor in the hand of his deputy, because the usurper is not a deputy for him, nor is the slave; rather the slave and what is in his hand are in the hand of the usurper, so it is not protected, so no liability. It becomes like stolen property in the hand of the thief after amputation. Also because the wage is a substitute for the benefit, and the substitute has the same ruling as the original; if he destroys the benefit he is not liable, so likewise its substitute. And what oscillates between two principles, we give it the benefit of both: we prefer the side of the master when the wage remains in his hand, so we say the master is more entitled to it; and we prefer the side of the usurper in regard to liability, so we say there is no liability upon him if he consumes the wage. Unlike the offspring of a usurped [female slave] where the usurper is liable for destroying it by transgression because it is not a substitute for benefit, but rather a part of the mother, so he is liable for it by transgression as for the mother. For this reason, if the usurper has a child by her, the child is not his; and if he hires out the slave, the wage is his [the usurper's].
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof passage detailing a dispute within the Hanafi school. The matn states: 'The usurper of a slave is not liable for what he consumes of his wage.' The commentary explains the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. According to Imam, when a usurper (ghasib) takes a slave, and the slave hires himself out, and the usurper takes the wage and consumes it, he is not liable for it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold he is liable. Imam's reasoning: liability only applies to property that is 'muhraz' (protected) and 'mutaqawwim' (valuable). The wage, while in the slave's hand, is under the control of the usurper (since he controls the slave), so it is not protected from him; thus it is like stolen property in the hand of a thief after amputation, which is no longer considered protected. Also, the wage is a substitute for the benefit of the slave; since the usurper is not liable for destroying the benefit (because he is a usurper and benefits are not compensable in Hanafi law), he is not liable for the substitute (the wage). The text then notes that when the wage is still in the hand of the slave, the master is more entitled to take it; but once consumed, the usurper is not liable. This is contrasted with the offspring of a usurped female slave, where the usurper is liable because the offspring is not a substitute for benefit but a part of the mother.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): One who wrongfully takes and controls another's property without permission.
محرز (muhraz): Property that is considered protected by being in the hand of its owner or his agent; it enjoys legal protection against usurpers.
بدل المنفعة (badal al-manfa'ah): Substitute for benefit, such as wage or rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is a usurper not liable for consuming the wage of the usurped slave?
A) Because the wage is considered the property of the usurper.
B) Because the wage is not 'muhraz' (protected) from the usurper since the slave is under his control.
C) Because the slave is not under interdiction.
D) Because the wage is less than a specific threshold.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a usurper is not liable for consuming the wage of a usurped slave because the wage is not considered protected property from the usurper, unlike the offspring of a female slave.

Owner's Right to Take Slave's Wage
Commentary explaining a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with qiyas proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave's wage belongs to his owner and explain the qiyas-based proof using stolen property analogy.

Core Idea
Ownership persists even when property loses legal protection; slave's wage belongs to owner.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if a slave earns wages, the owner may take them from the slave's hand. The commentator provides a qiyas (analogical) proof comparing it to stolen property after the thief's amputation. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi principle that ownership is not contingent on the property being 'mutaqawwim' (legally protected).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the owner taking the wage found in the slave's hand. The ruling is that it is permissible and the owner's right.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the case of stolen property after the thief's hand is amputated.
3
Analyze the qiyas structure
Identify the asl (original case): stolen property after amputation. Identify the far' (new case): wage in slave's hand. Identify the illah (effective cause): ownership persists despite loss of legal protection (taqawwum).
The illah is that ownership is separate from mutaqawwim status.
4
Apply the qiyas to derive the ruling
Just as the original owner retains ownership of stolen property and can take it, the slave owner retains ownership of the wage and can take it.
5
Note the principle established
The principle is that invalidity of taqawwum does not invalidate ownership. Ownership is a more fundamental right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو وجده ربه أخذه) يعني لو وجد رب العبد ما في يد العبد من الأجرة أخذه لأنه أخذ عين ماله ولا يلزم من بطلان التقوم بطلان الملك كما في المسروق بعد القطع فإنه لم يبق متقوماً حتى لا يضمن بالإتلاف ويبقى الملك فيه حتى يأخذه المالك.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if his owner finds it, he takes it) meaning: if the owner of the slave finds what is in the hand of the slave from the wage, he takes it because he is taking the very object of his property, and the invalidity of being valuable (taqawwum) does not necessitate the invalidity of ownership, as in the case of stolen property after amputation: it is no longer valuable such that one is liable for its destruction, but ownership remains in it until the owner takes it.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn statement: 'And if his owner finds it, he takes it.' The owner of a slave has the right to take any wages that are in the slave's hand, because the wage is his property. The author explains that even though the wage may not be considered 'mutaqawwim' (valuable/protected) in some contexts (such as being in the hand of a usurper or slave), that does not cancel the owner's ownership. He draws an analogy with stolen property after the thief's hand is amputated: the property is no longer considered valuable in the sense that the thief is not liable for destroying it, but the original owner still owns it and can reclaim it.

Key Terms
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that has legal value and is protected; destruction of such property incurs liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wage is like a gift to the slave.
B) The wage is like waqf property.
C) The wage is like stolen property after the thief's amputation.
D) The wage is like lost property (luqata).

Key Takeaway
Ownership is a more fundamental right than legal protection (taqawwum); the owner of property can reclaim it even when it is no longer mutaqawwim.

Validity of the Slave's Receipt of Wages: Consensus and Critical Redundancy
Commentary on a matn ruling and its critique of redundancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's receipt of wages, identify the proof of consensus (ijma'), and explain why the commentator considers this ruling repetitive.

Core Idea
The slave's receipt of wages is valid by consensus because he is the direct contracting party, but the commentator questions its repetition.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator addresses the matn's statement that a slave's receipt of his wage is valid. He affirms the ruling by consensus (ijma') and explains the rationale: the slave is the direct party to the contract and is permitted to engage in beneficial transactions. However, the commentator also notes that this ruling seems repetitive of an earlier passage concerning the mahjur (interdicted) slave, where the same conclusion was implied. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the nuance of the commentary's critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Determine the legal status of the slave's receipt of wages as stated in the matn and commentary.
The ruling is 'sahih' (valid).
2
Locate the Proof
Find the evidence cited for the ruling: 'by consensus (ijma')' because the slave is the direct party to the contract and is permitted in beneficial transactions.
Ijma' is a qat'i (definitive) source.
3
Analyze the Rationale
Understand why the slave's receipt is valid: the slave directly makes the contract, so the rights of the contract belong to him.
This distinguishes from a case where the contract is made by a guardian.
4
Examine the Critical Refinement
Identify the commentator's critique: the ruling is repetitive because the earlier passage (about the mahjur slave) already established the validity of the slave's receipt.
The commentator says it is 'repetition without benefit', urging reflection.
5
Assess the Repetition
Consider whether the repetition is truly without benefit or if it serves an additional purpose.
Some later commentators defend the repetition as clarifying a different aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وصح قبض العبد أجره) يعني لو قبض العبد الأجرة من المستأجر جاز قبضه بالإجماع لأنه المباشر للعقد وحقوق العقد إليه فيصح لكونه مأذوناً في التصرف النافع وهذه مكررة مع قوله ولا يأخذ مستأجر من عبد محجور إلى آخره لأنه أفاد صحة القبض الأخذ فهي تكرار بلا فائدة فتأمل.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the slave's receipt of the wage is valid) meaning: if the slave takes the wage from the hirer, his taking is valid by consensus because he is the direct party to the contract and the rights of the contract pertain to him, so it is valid because he is permitted in beneficial transactions. And this is repeated with his statement 'the hirer does not take from a slave under interdiction' etc., because it indicated the validity of receipt and taking, so it is a repetition without benefit, so reflect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This is a brief commentary on the matn: 'And the slave's receipt of the wage is valid.' The author notes that this ruling is by consensus (ijma'): the slave is the one who directly makes the contract, so he validly receives the wage. However, the author also comments that this is a repetition of the earlier ruling about the mahjur slave, where it was already established that the hirer cannot reclaim the wage, implying the validity of the slave's receipt. Therefore, he says it is repetition without benefit (takrar bila fa'ida), urging the student to reflect on why the author repeated it.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or receipt of property; in this context, the slave taking the wage from the hirer.
إجارة (ijara): Hire contract; the context of this ruling.
محجور (mahjur): A person under legal interdiction, unable to manage property freely; here a slave with restrictions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the master has given explicit permission.
C) Because the wage is for the master's benefit.
D) Because the contract is void otherwise.

Key Takeaway
The slave's receipt of wages is valid by consensus, even though the commentator finds the ruling redundant.

Hiring a Slave for Two Months at Different Rates
Commentary on a specific ijarah case with rate ambiguity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the first mentioned wage in a hire contract for consecutive periods applies to the first period immediately following the contract, and explain the reasoning by analogy with oaths and the principle of taharri.

Core Idea
When hiring for consecutive months at different rates, the first rate applies to the first month — contracts are interpreted to make them valid.

Hire contracts often involve multiple periods with different wages. How do we assign which wage to which period? In this passage, the commentator explains the Hanafi position using an analogy with oaths, resolving an ambiguity through the principle of striving for validity (taharri). The ruling is that the hire is valid and the first mentioned rate applies to the month immediately following the contract.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act: hiring a slave for two specific months at two different rates. The ruling is that the contract is valid and the first month is at the first mentioned rate (four).
2
Identify the reasoning (dalil)
The proof is an analogy with oaths: just as in an oath the action is considered immediately after the utterance, so in ijarah the first month is taken to be the one immediately after the contract. This is done 'taharrīyan lil-sihha' (striving for validity).
3
Examine the potential objection
A critic might argue that the phrase 'these two months' is definite, not indefinite, so the analogy with indefinite time periods in oaths does not apply.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The definite phrase refers back to the two indefinite months mentioned in the offer. The contract resolves the ambiguity by linking the first mentioned rate to the first period, just as if it were indefinite.
This is supported by al-Mabsut.
5
Appreciate the principle of taharri
The entire interpretation is driven by the goal of upholding the contract's validity. Ambiguities are resolved in favor of giving effect to the parties' intent both to hire and to specify different rates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو أجر عبده هذين الشهرين شهراً بأربعة وشهراً بخمسة صح والأول بأربعة لأنه لما قال أولاً شهراً بأربعة انصرف إلى ما يلي العقد تحرياً للصحة كما لو سكت عليه لأن الأوقات في حق الإجارة بمنزلة الأوقات في حق اليمين أن لا يكلم فلاناً لأن تنكرها مفسد فتعين عقبها، فإذا انصرف الأول إلى ما يليه انصرف الثاني تحرياً للأخير لأنه أقرب الأوقات إليه فصار كما لو صرح به قال تاج الشريعة : فإن قلت: هذا التعليل إنما يستقم إذا نكر الشهر وهنا عرف بقوله هذين قلت : رأيت في المبسوط وغيره : استأجر عبداً شهرين شهراً بأربعة وشهراً بخمسة فقال المستأجر استأجرت منك هذا العبد هذين الشهرين فينصرف قوله هذين الشهرين إلى الشهرين المنكرين .اهـ. وقال صاحب العناية : قيل مبنى الكلام على أنه ذكر منكراً مجهولاً والمذكور في الكتاب ليس كذلك. وأجيب بأن المذكور في الكتاب قول المستأجر واللام فيه للعهد لما في كلام المؤجر من التنكير فكان الأولى أن يقول «ولو قبل إجارة عبد إلى آخره فلو قال ذلك لكان أولى وكان يسلم من الاعتراض فتأمل.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he hires his slave these two months, one month for four and one month for five, it is valid, and the first is for four) because when he first said 'a month for four', it applies to the month immediately following the contract, striving for validity, just as if he was silent about it, because time periods in the matter of ijarah are like time periods in the matter of an oath that he shall not speak to so-and-so; because denying it corrupts, so it is determined immediately after it. So when the first applies to what follows it, the second applies to the last by striving, because it is the nearest time to it. So it becomes as if he explicitly stated it. Taj al-Shari'ah said: If you say: this reasoning is only sound if 'the month' is indefinite, but here it is definite by saying 'these two months'. I say: I saw in al-Mabsut and others: 'He hired a slave for two months, one month for four and one month for five, and the hirer said: I hired from you this slave these two months.' So his saying 'these two months' refers to the two indefinite months. And the author of al-Inayah said: It is said that the speech is based on mentioning an indefinite unknown, but what is mentioned in the book is not like that. And it was answered that what is mentioned in the book is the statement of the hirer, and the 'lam' in it is for the pact due to the indefiniteness in the statement of the offerer. So it would have been better if he said 'if before ijarah of a slave etc.' So if he said that, it would be better and would avoid objection. So reflect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn: 'If he hires his slave for these two months, one month for four and one month for five, it is valid, and the first is for four.' The commentary explains the reasoning: when the contract mentions 'one month for four' without specifying, it automatically applies to the first month after the contract, analogous to an oath where the time immediately after the oath is considered. Then the second month ('one month for five') applies to the next month. The text then discusses a potential objection by Taj al-Shari'ah: the reasoning assumes indefinite months, but the matn says 'these two months' which is definite. The answer is that the definite phrase refers to the two indefinite months mentioned earlier. The author of al-Inayah notes that the matn could have been phrased better, but the meaning is clear.

Key Terms
تحرياً للصحة (taharrīyan lil-sihha): Striving for validity by interpreting the contract in a way that makes it valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of hiring a slave for two months with different rates, which month is set at the first mentioned rate (four)?
A) The first month after the contract.
B) The second month after the contract.
C) The month of Muharram.
D) The month the slave chooses.

Key Takeaway
The first mentioned wage applies to the first period following the contract; this is based on the principle of interpreting contracts to be valid (taharri) and analogy with oaths.

Master's Right to Wage and Validity of Slave's Acceptance
Two rulings with proof by analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings regarding a slave's wage and explain the Hanafi proof that ownership persists independently of taqawwum.

Core Idea
Ownership of property does not depend on its legal value; the master retains ownership even if the slave holds the wage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq presents two interrelated rulings: the master's right to take the wage from his slave's hand, and the validity of the slave's acceptance of that wage. Both rulings hinge on the Hanafi distinction between ownership (milk) and legal valuation (taqawwum). By understanding this distinction, you will grasp why the master can reclaim the wage even though the slave's acceptance is perfectly valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate in the text the two separate rulings: (1) the master may take the wage found in the slave's hand, and (2) the slave's acceptance of the wage is valid.
The commentator explicitly states both.
2
Understand the proof for the master's right
The master takes because the wage is his own property ('ayn mālihi). The commentator argues that invalidity of taqawwum (legal value for liability) does not nullify ownership.
This is a qiyās: stolen property after amputation loses taqawwum but ownership remains.
3
Analyze the analogy to stolen property
Break down the analogy: asl = stolen property after hand cut (no taqawwum, yet owner can reclaim); far´ = wage in slave's hand (no taqawwum because slave cannot own, yet master owns). Both share the ´illa: ownership persists despite loss of legal value.
This is a dalīl ´aqlī (rational proof).
4
State the basis for the slave's valid acceptance
The slave's acceptance is valid by consensus (ijmā´) because he is the direct party to the hire contract and is authorized to engage in beneficial transactions.
The commentator notes this is repetitive but included for clarity.
5
Recognize the commentator's note on repetition
The commentator observes that the ruling on slave's acceptance is repeated from an earlier statement; reflect on why the author might include it again.
Reflect on the pedagogical purpose: emphasis on a key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو وجده ربه أخذه) يعني لو وجد رب العبد ما في يد العبد من الأجرة أخذه لأنه أخذ عين ماله ولا يلزم من بطلان التقوم بطلان الملك كما في المسروق . بعد القطع فإنه لم يبق متقوماً حتى لا يضمن بالإتلاف ويبقى الملك فيه حتى يأخذه المالك قال رحمه الله : وصح قبض العبد أجره) يعني لو قبض العبد الأجرة من المستأجر جاز قبضه بالإجماع لأنه المباشر للعقد وحقوق العقد إليه فيصح لكونه مأذوناً في التصرف النافع وهذه مكررة مع قوله ولا يأخذ مستأجر من عبد محجور إلى آخره لأنه أفاد صحة القبض الأخذ فهي تكرار بلا فائدة فتأمل.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if his master finds him, he takes it) meaning if the master of the slave finds in the slave's hand the wage, he takes it because he is taking his own property, and the invalidity of being considered property (taqawwum) does not necessitate the invalidity of ownership, just like with stolen property after amputation. After amputation, the property is no longer considered valuable such that one is liable for destruction, but ownership remains until the owner takes it. He (the author) said: (And the slave's acceptance of his wage is valid) meaning if the slave accepts the wage from the hirer, his acceptance is valid by consensus because he is the direct party to the contract and the rights of the contract pertain to him, so it is valid because he is permitted to engage in beneficial transactions. This is repetitive with his statement: 'And the hirer should not take from a restricted slave...' because it states the validity of acceptance and taking, so it is repetition without benefit—reflect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. First, the ruling that if the master finds his slave in possession of the wage the slave earned through hiring himself out, the master may take it. The reasoning is that the wage is still the master's property; even if the slave's hand makes the wage non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) for liability purposes, ownership remains. The analogy is stolen property after the thief's hand is amputated: the thief is not liable for destruction because the property is no longer considered valuable, but the owner still owns it and can reclaim it. Second, the ruling that the slave's acceptance of the wage from the hirer is valid. This is by consensus because the slave is the direct party to the hire contract and is authorized to perform beneficial transactions. The commentator notes that this ruling is repetitive with an earlier statement, but still included for completeness.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): Legal consideration of an item as property (māl mutaqawwim) for purposes of liability and compensation.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical or legal acceptance/possession of an item, often required for transfer of liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the master take the wage from the slave's hand even though the slave had accepted it?
A) Because the slave is not legally capable of accepting wages
B) Because the wage remains the master's property, and ownership is not lost by the slave's possession
C) Because the hirer must pay the master directly
D) Because the slave's acceptance is void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership (milk) persists even when an item loses legal value (taqawwum), allowing the master to reclaim the wage from his slave while the slave's acceptance remains valid.

Judging by the Current Situation in Hire Disputes
Explains the ḥukm al-ḥāl procedure and its justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ḥukm al-ḥāl to determine whose statement is accepted in a dispute and explain why apparent evidence suffices for defense but not for establishing liability.

Core Idea
In disputes, the court judges by the current apparent situation — the one supported by the visible evidence is believed, but only to repel a claim, not to establish liability.

When a hirer and an owner of a slave disagree about whether the slave absconded or fell ill during the hire period, how does the court decide whom to believe? The Hanafi school relies on the current situation (ḥukm al-ḥāl): the party whose current appearance supports his claim is believed, provided he takes an oath. This lesson unpacks this procedural rule, its justification, and its limits, drawing on the commentary from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that the disagreement is about a fact (absence or illness) during the hire period, and note the two possible claims: the hirer says the event occurred, the owner denies it or claims it began before the hire.
The parties agree on the wage amount but differ on obligation (wujūb al-ujra).
2
Understand the principle of ḥukm al-ḥāl
The court looks at the present state (e.g., whether the slave is currently absent or present) and accepts the statement of the person whose current situation supports his claim, but only with an oath.
The apparent evidence (ẓāhir) serves as a preponderator (murajjiḥ), not conclusive proof.
3
Apply to the hirer-favored scenario
If the current situation supports the hirer (e.g., slave is absent), his word is taken to repel the owner's claim for wage, because the hirer is defending against an obligation.
The apparent suffices for defense (dafʿ al-istīḥqāq).
4
Analyze the owner-favored scenario
If the current situation supports the owner (e.g., slave is present), the owner is believed because his entitlement to the wage is based on the original contract (the prior cause) and the apparent only indicates its continuity.
The owner is not entitled solely by the apparent; the contract itself establishes the obligation.
5
Extend to analogous cases
Recognize that the same principle applies in other disputes, such as freeing a slave woman with a child, or selling palm trees with fruit — the word is given to the one in possession because the apparent supports him.
These examples reinforce that ḥukm al-ḥāl operates in diverse contexts but always within the same logic of defense vs. entitlement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو اختلفا في اباق العبد ومرضه حكم الحال يعني لو استأجر عبداً شهراً مثلاً ثم قال المستأجر في آخر الشهر أبق أو مرض في المدة وأنكر المولى ذلك أو أنكر استناده إلى أول المدة فقال أصابه قبل من تأتيني بساعة يحكم الحال فيكون القول قول من شهد له الحال مع يمينه لأن القول في الدعاوى قول من يشهد له الظاهر ووجوده في الحال يدل على وجوده في الماضي فيصلح الظاهر مرجحاً، وإن لم يصلح حجة كما إذا اختلفا في جريان ماء الطاحون. وهذا إذا كان الظاهر يشهد للمستأجر فظاهر لأنه لا إشكال فيه لأنه ليس فيه إلا دفع الاستحقاق والظاهر يصلح له، فإن كان يشهد للمؤجر ففيه إشكال من حيث إنه يستحق الأجرة بالظاهر وهو لا يصلح للاستحقاق، وجوابه أنه يستحق بالسبب السابق وهو وإنما الظاهر يشهد على بقائه واستمراره إلى ذلك الوقت فلم يكن مستحقاً بمجرد الظاهر، وهذا لأنهما لما اتفقا على وجود سبب الوجوب فقد أقر بالوجوب عليه، وإذا أنكره يكون متعرضاً لنفيه فلا يقبل إلا بحجة وعلى هذا لو أعتق جارية ولها ولد فقالت أعتقتني قبل ولادتي فهو حر وقال المولى أعتقتها بعده فهو رقيق فالقول قول من الولد في يده لأن الظاهر يشهد له، وكذا لو باع نخلاً فيه ثمرة واختلفا في الثمرة معها كان القول قول من في يده الثمرة. وهذا كله إذا اتفقا على قدر الأجرة واختلفا في الوجوب
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if they differ about the slave's absconding or illness, the current situation is judged.) Meaning if someone hires a slave for a month, and at the end of the month the hirer says 'He absconded or fell ill during the period,' and the owner denies that or denies it relates to the beginning of the period, saying 'It happened before he came to me an hour ago,' then the current situation is judged: the word is accepted from the one whom the current situation supports, with his oath. Because in claims, the word is from the one whom the apparent evidence supports, and his current existence indicates his past existence, so the apparent evidence serves as a preponderator, even if it is not conclusive proof, as when they differ about the flow of the mill water. This applies when the apparent supports the hirer; it is clear because there is no issue except repelling the claim, and the apparent suffices for that. If it supports the owner, there is an issue because he claims the wage by the apparent, which is not sufficient for entitlement. The answer is that he is entitled by the prior cause, and the apparent only indicates its continuation to that time, so he is not entitled merely by the apparent. This is because when they agree on the existence of the cause of obligation, he has admitted obligation upon himself, and if he denies it, he is attempting to negate it, so he is not accepted without evidence. Similarly, if someone frees a female slave who has a child, and she says 'You freed me before my birth,' so he is free, and the owner says 'I freed her after his birth,' so he is a slave, then the word is from the one in whose hand the child is because the apparent supports him. Likewise, if someone sells palm trees with fruit and they differ about the fruit, the word is from the one in whose hand the fruit is. This all applies when they agree on the amount of the wage and differ on obligation.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on a dispute between the hirer and the owner of a slave regarding whether the slave absconded or fell ill during the hire period. The principle is that the court examines the current situation (ḥukm al-ḥāl) and accepts the statement of the party whose current appearance supports him, with an oath. For example, if the slave is currently absent, that supports the hirer's claim that the slave absconded. The commentator explains that the apparent evidence is sufficient to overcome a claim, though not enough to establish liability. A difficulty arises when the owner claims the wage based on the fact that the slave was well at the start, but the answer is that the owner's entitlement is based on the original contract, and the apparent only confirms its continuation. The commentary then provides analogous cases: a freed slave woman with a child, and a sale of palm trees with fruit, where the word goes to the one in possession because the apparent supports him. This section ends with 'This all applies when they agree on the amount of the wage and differ on obligation,' transitioning to the next topic.

Key Terms
حكم الحال (ḥukm al-ḥāl): Judging based on the current apparent situation to determine whose statement is credible in a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the hirer claims the slave absconded during the hire period and the owner denies it, who is believed if the slave is currently absent?
A) The owner, because he is the owner
B) The hirer, because the current absence supports his claim
C) Both must provide conclusive proof
D) The slave is believed

Key Takeaway
In disputes over factual events like a slave's absence, the court decides by the current situation, accepting the statement of the party supported by the apparent evidence after an oath, but this procedural tool does not resolve substantive liability disputes.

Resolving Wage Disputes in Ijarah: Evidence and Value Increase
Rulings on disputes about wage amount, conflicting testimonies, and oaths in hire contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on evidence and value increase to determine the correct wage in a dispute between a worker and a hirer.

Core Idea
When wage is disputed, evidence decides; if none, value increase determines who is believed.

This lesson examines a passage from later Hanafi sources detailing how a qadi resolves a dispute over the amount of wages in an ijarah contract. The key principle is that if one party provides evidence (bayyinah), it prevails; if both do, the worker's evidence is preferred. If neither has evidence, the court assesses the increase in value caused by the work—the party whose claim is supported by that increase is believed, after an oath. This lesson will walk you through the analytical steps to apply these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine the type of dispute: (a) amount of wage, (b) type of benefit, (c) leased item, (d) distance, etc. This lesson focuses on wage amount (the dyer and garment owner example).
The passage also covers other disputes; we focus on wage.
2
Check if any party has evidence (bayyinah)
If one party produces evidence, that evidence is accepted. If both produce evidence, the worker's evidence (dyer) is preferred. If neither has evidence, proceed to step 3.
Evidence means two male witnesses or one male and two females.
3
Assess the increase in value
If no evidence, look at how much the work increased (or decreased) the value of the subject matter (e.g., garment). Compare this increase with the claims of the parties.
If the work decreased value, the hirer's statement is taken.
4
Apply the rule based on value comparison
If increase >= worker's claim, worker is believed after oath. If increase < hirer's claim, hirer is believed after oath. If increase in between, worker gets the amount equal to increase after oath.
The worker's oath is that he did not do it for the lower amount.
5
Consider special cases: before vs after receipt
If the dispute arises before the benefit is received (i.e., before the work is done), witness disagreements about the wage invalidate the testimony. After receipt, different rules apply (not detailed here).
This prevents premature litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو اختلفا في قدر الأجرة واتفقا في الوجوب قال في فتاوى قاضيخان ولو اختلفا في الأجر فقال الصباغ عملته بدرهم وقال صاحب الثوب بدانقين فأيهما أقام البينة قبلت بينته وإن أقاماها فبينة الصباغ، وإن لم يكن لهما بينة ينظر ما زاد الصبغ في قيمة الثوب، فإن كان درهماً أو أكثر يؤخذ بقول الصباغ فيعطي درهماً بعد يمينه بالله ما صبغه بدانقين، وإن كان ما زاد الصبغ فيه أقل من دانقين كان القول قول رب الثوب مع يمينه على ما ادعى الصباغ، فإن كان يزيد في قيمة الثوب نصف درهم يعطى لصباغ نصف درهم مع يمينه كما تقدم، وإن كان ينقص الصبغ الثوب كان القول قول صاحب الثواب ا هـ . قال في المحيط وغيره: وإذا اختلف شاهد الأجرة في مقدارها إن كانت الحاجة إلى القضاء بالعقد قبل استيفاء المعقود عليه فالشهادة باطلة، سواء كان يدعي أقل المالين أو أكثرهما، فإن كانت الحاجة إلى القضاء بالدين بأن وقع الاختلاف بعد الاستيفاء وقد تقدم ولو اختلفا في نفس المنفعة فشهد أحدهما بالركوب والآخر بالحمل أو قال أحدهما بزعفران وقال الآخر بعصفر لم تقبل الشهادة. هذا إن اتفقا على العين المؤجرة، فلو اختلفا فيها قال في المحيط : لو اختلفا في العين المؤجرة بأن قال المؤجر أجرتك هذه الدابة وقال المستأجر بل هذه يتحالفان ولو اختلفا في جنس الأجرة وأقاما البينة وكل بينة تثبت الزيادة تقبل بينة كل فيما يدعيه، ولو اختلفا في المسافة فقال أحدهما مثلاً في ديارنا إلى الخانكا وقال الآخر إلى بلبيس يتحالفان وأيهما قام البيئة تقبل بينته وإن أقاماها جميعاً أخذ ببينة رب الدابة في إثبات الأجرة وببيئة المستأجر في إثبات زيادة المسافة .
If they differ about the amount of the wage and agree on the obligation, Qadikhan said in his Fatawa: And if they differ about the wage, and the dyer says 'I did the work for a dirham,' and the owner of the garment says 'for two daniqs,' then whichever of them produces evidence, his evidence is accepted; if both produce evidence, the evidence of the dyer is preferred. If neither has evidence, then we look at how much the dye increased the value of the garment. If it was a dirham or more, the dyer's statement is taken, and he is given a dirham after his oath by Allah that he did not do it for two daniqs. If the increase was less than two daniqs, the statement of the garment owner is taken with his oath regarding what the dyer claimed. If the increase in the value of the garment is half a dirham, the dyer is given half a dirham with his oath as previously. If the dye decreases the value of the garment, the statement of the garment owner is taken. End. Al-Muhit and others said: If witnesses to the wage differ about its amount, if the need is to rule on the contract before the hired benefit is received, the testimony is invalid, whether the witness claims the lesser or greater. If the need is to rule on the debt, i.e., the disagreement occurred after receipt, [it is different]. And if they differ about the very benefit, one witness testifies to riding and another to carrying, or one says saffron and another says 'usfur, the testimony is not accepted. This applies if they agree on the leased item. If they differ on the leased item, al-Muhit says: If they differ on the leased item, e.g., the lessor says 'I leased this donkey to you,' and the lessee says 'rather this one,' they take an oath. If they differ on the type of wage and produce evidence, each evidence is accepted for what it claims. If they differ on the distance, e.g., one says 'to our land, to al-Khankah,' and the other says 'to Bilbays,' they take an oath, and whichever produces evidence, his evidence is accepted; if both produce evidence, the evidence of the donkey owner is taken regarding the wage, and the evidence of the hirer regarding the increased distance.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings from Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Muhit on disputes in ijarah contracts. The primary scenario is when the amount of the wage is disputed but both parties agree that payment is due. The example given is a dyer and the garment owner. The ruling: if one party produces evidence, it prevails; if both do, the dyer's evidence is preferred (because he is the worker). If neither has evidence, the court looks at the increase in value from the work. If the increase equals or exceeds the dyer's claimed wage, the dyer is believed with oath; if it is less than the owner's claim, the owner is believed; if the work decreased the value, the owner is believed. The passage then discusses cases where witnesses differ on the wage before the benefit is received—their testimony is invalid due to uncertainty. If the dispute occurs after the benefit is received, the rules change (not detailed here). Further, if witnesses differ on the type of benefit (riding vs. carrying) or the substance (saffron vs. 'usfur), testimony is not accepted. If the parties differ on the specific leased item (e.g., which donkey), they take an oath. If they differ on the type of wage (e.g., currency vs. goods) and produce evidence, each evidence is accepted. If they differ on the distance of transport and both produce evidence, the donkey owner's evidence on the wage is taken, and the hirer's evidence on the distance is taken.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between a dyer and a garment owner where neither has evidence, and the dye increased the value by less than two daniqs, who is believed?
A) The dyer
B) The garment owner
C) Both take an oath
D) The court appoints an appraiser

Key Takeaway
When wage is disputed and no evidence, the increase in value serves as an objective basis to determine who is believed.

Whose Word Counts? The Owner vs. Craftsman Dispute in Hanafi Law
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence in owner-craftsman disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on evidence in owner-craftsman disputes and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Custom overrides default: the relied-upon fatwa accepts the craftsman's word if he is known in his trade.

When an owner and a craftsman disagree about the work done, whose statement is taken as true? Imam Abu Hanifa gave the owner the benefit of the doubt, but his disciples introduced practical exceptions based on custom. This lesson breaks down the arguments and shows why the later fatwa adopts Muhammad al-Shaybani's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenarios
The text lists three types: garment type (shirt/cloak), dye colour (red/yellow), and whether a wage was agreed. All stem from the owner's permission.
2
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa's default rule
The owner's word is accepted because he gave permission and knows best what he ordered. Denial of a description follows denial of the permission itself.
Qiyas supports this.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's condition of habitual dealing
If the dyer is a regular worker (harif) of the owner, his word is taken. Otherwise the owner's.
Based on custom.
4
Examine Muhammad al-Shaybani's condition of known trade
If the dyer is known in the dyeing trade for a wage, his word prevails. Opening a shop implies that.
This is istihsan.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
The fatwa is on Muhammad's view due to prevalent custom. In practice, if the craftsman is known for that craft, his description is accepted.
The owner's oath is required if the craftsman's description is not reliable.
